How American Industry Wins the AI Era | AIPCon 9 Discussion

Palantir(146.0K) ·34:34 ·3.8K views · 2026-03-24
Shyam Sankar (CTO, Palantir) Mike Gallagher (Head of Defense, Palantir) Cameron Stanley (Chief Digital & AI Officer, Dow)
> "The factory is the deterrent.
TL;DR: To dominate the AI era, the United States must reintegrate its industrial base with its national security goals, shifting from a peacetime "compliance" mindset to an "outcome-led" war footing that prioritizes decision dominance, domestic energy independence, and...

TL;DR: To dominate the AI era, the United States must reintegrate its industrial base with its national security goals, shifting from a peacetime "compliance" mindset to an "outcome-led" war footing that prioritizes decision dominance, domestic energy independence, and high-agency talent.

Re-Industrializing the "Factory-to-Foxhole" Pipeline

  • The Factory as Deterrent: Palantir CTO Shyam Sankar argues that national security is underpinned by the broader American industrial base, not just defense contractors. He posits that the ability to manufacture at scale is the ultimate deterrent, noting that innovation is a direct consequence of high productivity.
  • Strategic Re-Industrialization: The U.S. is moving back toward an industrial model where innovative, tech-forward companies build in the national interest across multiple sectors (energy, aerospace, etc.).
  • Linkage Deficit: The primary missing strategic asset is the real-time data linkage between the factory floor and the active "foxhole," allowing production to adapt to battlefield realities instantly.

Securing Vulnerable Strategic Domains: Nuclear and Stratosphere

  • Nuclear Supply Chain Risk: Amir Vexler (CEO, Centrus Energy) highlights that the U.S. currently imports 70% of its nuclear enrichment needs, with 20% of the grid dependent on this fuel.
      • Adversary Dependence: The U.S. has lost the domestic capability to enrich uranium for commercial and defense needs over decades of competition with state-subsidized foreign entities.
      • Scaling Requirements: To secure energy independence, the U.S. must scale from 16 centrifuges to 11,000, requiring a complete reconstruction of a dormant supply chain.
  • Stratospheric Dominance: Ryan Hartman (CEO, World View) emphasizes the stratosphere as a critical domain for Intelligence, Surveillance, and Reconnaissance (ISR).
      • Persistent Monitoring: Stratospheric balloons offer 30+ days of continuous "pattern of life" monitoring at a lower cost than satellites or manned aircraft.
      • The China Gap: China is currently manufacturing and deploying these systems at a scale that exceeds U.S. production, necessitating faster transitions from prototypes to operational capabilities.

Achieving Decision Dominance in the "Department of War"

  • Data-Centric Lethality: Cameron Stanley (CDAO, Department of War) argues that "decision dominance" is achieved by treating every member of the military as a data professional.
  • From Monoliths to Interconnectivity: The Joint All-Domain Command and Control (JADC2) vision has shifted. Instead of "one system to rule them all," the focus is now on interconnecting best-of-breed systems via common ontologies and data structures.
  • The Speed of AI: Modern workflows—such as Maven Smart System—now operate in milliseconds. This allows strategic leaders in the Pentagon to observe and direct tactical actions happening half a world away in real-time.

The Talent War: Prioritizing Agency Over Credentials

  • Aptitude vs. Education: Panelists agreed that in rapidly evolving fields like stratospheric ballooning or nuclear enrichment, specific degrees often don't exist.
      • Hiring for Agency: Companies are increasingly hiring for "attitude and aptitude"—the internal belief that one can figure out complex problems—rather than traditional credentials.
      • Software as an Enabler: Advanced software platforms (like Palantir’s AIP) allow workers with high school diplomas to perform tasks that previously required advanced degrees by automating technical complexity and focusing the human on judgment.
  • Bridging the Gov-Industry Divide: A significant barrier to progress is the lack of mutual understanding regarding budgeting and milestones between the private sector and government bureaucrats.

Adopting "Quantum" Organizational Structures

  • Fluidity over Hierarchy: Shyam Sankar suggests that successful organizations in the AI era must be "quantum"—capable of being hierarchical, matrixed, or flat depending on the specific problem of the moment.
  • The Primacy of Winning: Peacetime thinking leads to ossified, compliance-led structures. A "war footing" requires decentralized, high-agency leadership that prioritizes outcomes over process.

***

"Aptitude and attitude over experience and education every single time... I always look past the resume... if you're clever and you're driven from a mission perspective, I can put you to good use." — Cameron Stanley, CDAO, Department of War

And now now they're some of the best professionals in the data data world working on these problems. So I think talent can be grown organically. Yes, industry plays plays a role here. Shyam, you're part of this. Yeah, there's a way in which what's old is new again. So if you think about our education system really through high school it was developed in the industrial age to service industrial companies and originally it was built by the companies themselves and then kind of institutionalized. We need to reimagine this in the information age. And I think one of the things that's most different here, and by the way the military's always had to do this, like whatever you you know you go to college you still go to basic, you go to advanced individual training after that. So the military always had to have some mix of schoolhouse and apprenticeship to make you good at this very unique set of jobs, military occupation specialties that you'd be ending up in. At the dawn of the information age here, maybe slightly past the dawn, but early morning of the information age, we we need to optimize on agency. You know there are a lot of things that technology's going to help us do. The one thing it is not going to do and the one thing that we actually optimized against in the industrial age is agency. And it is attitude and aptitude is one way of thinking about agency. Like do you believe you can figure it out even though you've never written Python? You know, do you believe in yourself you have that ability? It's more than just grit. It's it's this internal sense that I can bend the arc of history and the world through my efforts. I think it absolutely does and just to flush that out for for the audience here, you know you could say should the right stru- you know should we be hierarchical, should we be matrixed, should we be flat? And then tomorrow there's going to be a different problem that matters and you need to have the flexibility to reorganize and crystallize state around that. Like is it a wave, is it a particle? I don't know, depends on when you're observing it. It's both. It's neither. And so similarly I think with government you look at some of the most exquisite parts of the military, JSOC you would say, "Oh this is what they do. They call it task organizing." Yeah. Christine Lagarde - President of the European Central Bank | Podcast | In Good Company

Christine Lagarde (President, European Central Bank) Nicolai Tangen (CEO, Norges Bank Investment Management)

TL;DR Christine Lagarde warns that the current global landscape mirrors the 1920s, characterized by transformative technological breakthroughs and geopolitical fragmentation; she argues that Europe must accelerate its green transition and AI integration to preserve its social...

TL;DR

Christine Lagarde warns that the current global landscape mirrors the 1920s, characterized by transformative technological breakthroughs and geopolitical fragmentation; she argues that Europe must accelerate its green transition and AI integration to preserve its social democratic model amid demographic shifts and energy vulnerabilities.

Geopolitical Fragmentation and the "1920s Analogy"

  • Historical Parallel: Lagarde likens the current era to the 1920s, noting that both periods featured rapid technological shifts (AI today vs. the combustion engine then) occurring alongside a retreat from globalization.
      • Risks of Escalation: She warns that failing to manage current fragmentation through diplomacy and sound financial management could lead to a repeat of the 1930s—financial crises followed by global conflict.
  • European Vulnerability: As one of the world’s most open economies, Europe is uniquely exposed to trade shocks and disruptions in global shipping routes, specifically the Strait of Hormuz.
  • The Energy Nexus: The Middle East conflict highlights Europe's lack of domestic energy resources.
      • Strategic Pivot: While high volatility in oil and gas prices creates short-term strain, Lagarde argues it must serve as a catalyst to accelerate the green transition and increase nuclear energy capacity to ensure long-term sovereignty.

Economic Productivity and the AI Revolution

  • The Productivity Gap: Europe missed the "internet revolution," leading to a decades-long divergence in productivity compared to the United States.
  • AI Opportunities: While the US currently leads in AI *pioneering* (capital, data, and chips), Lagarde believes Europe can compete in AI *diffusion*.
      • Manufacturing Edge: European SMEs are currently ahead of US counterparts in integrating AI into manufacturing processes and service delivery.
      • Speed of Adoption: Lagarde notes the unprecedented speed of AI; while electricity took 30 years to diffuse, AI tools like ChatGPT reached mass adoption in under three.
  • Deflationary Potential: While AI should theoretically be deflationary by boosting productivity, the massive energy costs of training Large Language Models (LLMs) and geopolitical fragmentation may offset these gains.

The ECB Mandate and Monetary Lessons

  • Price Stability Priority: Lagarde emphasizes that the ECB’s singular mandate is price stability, which requires total independence from the short-term "electoral timelines" of politicians.
  • Post-COVID Reflections: She admits the ECB was "a bit slow" to recognize inflationary pressures in 2022.
      • Forward Guidance Regrets: Lagarde expresses regret over feeling overly bound by previous "forward guidance" rather than reacting more nimbly to shifting data sensors.
  • The Digital Euro: Anticipated for a pilot phase in 2027 and a full rollout by 2029, the digital euro is framed as a "public good" necessary to keep central bank money relevant for younger generations who no longer carry cash.
      • Stablecoin Skepticism: Lagarde remains unconvinced of the business case for stablecoins, viewing them primarily as bridges between the crypto and fiat worlds rather than essential financial instruments.

Leadership and the European Social Model

  • The Cost of Democracy: Lagarde defends the principles of European social democracy but acknowledges that demographic aging and increased military spending may require "reduced benefits" or higher retirement ages to remain sustainable.
  • Diversity of Thought: She warns against "corridor thinking" or groupthink within institutions.
      • The Value of Outliers: Lagarde intentionally includes "outliers" (people of different backgrounds, genders, or professional expertise) in her decision-making groups to challenge the status quo, even if it creates "irritation" within the team.
  • Leadership Style: Transitioning from a lawyer and Finance Minister to the ECB, Lagarde describes herself as an "inclusive leader" who has become increasingly impatient with the slow pace of institutional change.
"I think that we have to be informed by history and try to avoid what came after those developments in the 20s that ended up with... a global conflict that destroyed many of the advanced economies." — Christine Lagarde
"I’m a very strong believer of the value of diversity of thinking... When you put people having gone to the same schools, graduated from the same group of universities... having focused on the same issues, worked on the same models, all of them together—I think that is dangerous." — Christine Lagarde
"If we have to referee and trade off between military expenses... and if we have to have less of those social benefits of which cost accrues and increases as the demographic changes take place in Europe... I think we should face it and decide what we want collectively." — Christine Lagarde
Hi everyone, I'm Nicolai Tangen, the CEO of the Norwegian Sovereign Wealth Fund, and today we are in particularly good company because we are in Frankfurt with Christine Lagarde, President of the European Central Bank. Lovely to see you, Nicolai, and welcome to Frankfurt. Thank you. Now, Christine, you've been—you had a tremendous career. You were running a global law firm, you've been the finance minister of France, you ran the IMF for a decade, and now you are steering the monetary policy of Europe. So thanks for seeing us. That's a great pleasure. I heard you recently say that the world now looks a bit like 1929. What is similar? I think the analogy I made was with the 20s, because it's a time when there were major technology breakthroughs that of course we take for granted, but which were new at the time—you know, the combustion engine, the manufacturing line, all sorts of things that just came about together at a time when fragmentation started to also significantly change the way the world worked. This was preceded by a period of open trade and the first globalization, if you will. And we are seeing a bit of that at the moment. There is a technological breakthrough, which I would associate with the development of AI and the diffusion of AI on the one hand, and fragmentation challenges to the international world order as we have known it for decades. So that's the analogy. And I think that we have to be informed by history and try to avoid what came after those developments in the 20s that ended up with, number one, financial crisis, bankruptcies of banks in Europe, and eventually, because the matter was not handled very well at the time, a global conflict that destroyed many of the advanced economies and made all of us at the time poorer and fewer. What do you think are the implications for financial markets then? Because we know what happened at the end of the 20s. I think the implications for policymakers of all stripes means putting the onus on diplomacy rather than war, the onus on good financial management of public budgets, and being attentive to the real transformation and not so much hype. That's what I would see as favorable developments. The fragmentation we are seeing in the world just now—how does it impact Europe? Europe is of all advanced economies probably the most open. This is very true for some of the Nordic countries that you know well, but it's true for the whole of Europe. So any shock to trade, for instance, is going to impact an open economy more than an economy that is not self-sufficient but not particularly vulnerable to trade, whether export or import. Added to that, Europe has very limited own resources and energy sources, with the exception of the renewable energies that we are pushing and the green transition that we are also trying to finance as a continent. But other than that, sources of energy are rare, and that exposes us significantly in the current situation. What's the added complexity from what we are seeing in the Middle East? Well, I would say the constraint—apart from the horrible drama that afflicts the life of people, the situation of families, the civilian population that eventually end up being the target of those wars. So putting that aside, which is a big put aside, it impacts the global economy in that energy sources like oil and gas are under threat in terms of transportation and shipping. The Strait of Hormuz comes to mind right away, and as a result of that, we see prices increasing significantly. We see a volatility that is unprecedented in the last few decades, where suddenly the price of oil can go up by 30 percent and go down by 30 percent in a matter of one day. So that's what the Middle East current war development is creating for the rest of us: disruption in shipping, disruption in insurance costs, and obviously significant increases in the price of energy, which as we know, is necessary for all economies and has ripple effects in a matter of months. What's the right way for Europe to deal with all this uncertainty? I think you have to distinguish between the short term and the long term. In the long term, it is in Europe's best interest to focus on that green transition that was identified a few years ago by the European Commission and the European Council, which comprises all the leaders of Europe. It's actually fascinating to see how only a few weeks ago, this was almost put on the back burner as if it was not a priority. Clearly, what has happened in the Middle East is bringing this priority back to the front of the mind of leaders. This green transition that would increase the volume of renewable energies versus the dependency on fossil energy is a long term objective that has to be pursued and at an accelerated pace. In the short term, you know, obviously every effort should be made in order to reopen the Strait of Hormuz and facilitate the passing of these 20-25 percent of oil traffic that goes through that particular strait. I think there is, you know, it's neither nor, but the focus on nuclear energies is also one that we will see developing faster in Europe as well. Now there's been some changes in policymaking in the US, and we attended the same opening dinner in Davos. You were sitting at a table next to where I was, and you didn't seem very happy at the time. So what went through your mind? You could see that? I'm very bad at covering up and having a poker face. So what I thought was not acceptable is the fact that a US government representative, without contradiction and without any kind of dialogue, could at the end of a dinner which had the participation of many European leaders, including royalty and representatives of those countries—that he could just harangue and bash Europe in the way he did. And I thought, okay, well, all opinions are welcome as long as there is a debate, but the way in which this came about was to me not acceptable, so I left the room. Was there some truth in what he said? I think that every economy, every individual, and every corporate has elements of vulnerabilities and of being wrong, and Europe does not escape that particular judgment passing, if you will. But to bash the green transition, to bash the wind energy or the solar energy in the way it was done, I thought was just not in the cards. Do you think we can afford the type of system that we've had in Europe for the last decades, going forward? I think it's imperative for Europe and for the Europeans to appreciate value and vote for the value delivered by social democracies. Does that mean that the entire social benefits can be maintained as is with the same financing and the same benefits? That is to be assessed, determined, and possibly modified over the course of time, given the demographic challenge that we are facing. But the principles of democracy, the independence, the freedom of speech, the individual rights, and the economic development at an entrepreneurial level that it facilitates—I think those are precious, precious principles that we have to secure. And if it requires reduced benefits in some areas, I think we should be prepared to consider that. If we have to referee and trade off between military expenses because we want to secure that precious treasure that has been acquired over the course of time and generations, and if we have to have less of those social benefits of which cost accrues and increases as the demographic changes take place in Europe—aging societies, less working population—I think we should face it and decide what we want collectively. So to which extent has your job become more complicated, or the job as the President of the ECB become more complicated with the change in geopolitics, demographics, and defense spending? There are just a lot more things going into the mix than in the past, no? Well, we are lucky that we are driven by a mandate, which I regard as the mission of the ECB, which is price stability. That's our duty to the people of Europe. We have to make sure that prices remain stable and that people can decide to invest, to consume, and to be employed in a landscape where prices are stable. That's the mission. And to deliver on that mission, we have to take into account all the developments that affect European economies, and that has internal elements and external elements. The geopolitical shocks are an external element that has ramifications in our economies. The internal elements, such as demographics for instance, such as the impact of artificial intelligence, such as the rise in productivity—we have to take more and more factors into account in order to determine how we deliver on the mission. That is true, and it makes our life more complicated, and it requires a very strong focus and strong analytical skills in order to deliver. Do you think your background as a lawyer, as a finance minister, at the IMF, and so on, has been particularly useful in putting all this together? The answer is yes, for two reasons. I think having been a lawyer has taught me how to listen. It has taught me how to separate the key arguments from the vicinity—the signals from the noise, translated into economic terms. And that is very helpful. I think being a lawyer has also taught me a little bit of humility, because you're only as good as your last advocacy, your last pleading, and ultimately the magistrates will decide. I think having been a minister of trade, agriculture briefly, and finance for a longer period of time, has also taught me a bit about the intricacies and the trade-offs in politics. And at the end of the day, things happen as a result of political development, leaders taking a view, making a move, or deciding a new policy. Understanding how that fabric works has been very helpful. The IMF gave me a very broad view of what is happening in the world and has exposed me, together with the years as Minister of Finance, to very talented economists. So you could argue, and I take this argument happily, that not having been an economist for enough of my life, I'm vulnerable in my assessment and the decision-making that I go through. And I would challenge that, because I'm surrounded, and I'm so privileged to be surrounded by extremely talented economists of all stripes and colors. Do you think being an economist as a métier is overrated? I wouldn't say it's overrated. I tell you what I think is overrated. It's what I call the corridor thinking, the groupthink. When you put people having gone to the same schools, graduated from the same group of universities, having focused on the same issues, worked on the same models, all of them together—I think that is dangerous. And are they particularly susceptible to that? I think any profession is susceptible to that. If you bring about a group of lawyers and you leave them amongst themselves—you bring banking law experts—they can talk ad nauseam about a particular thing, but they will not see the ramification in terms of competition law, the impact on economics, or the financial consequences of what they do. So I'm a very strong believer in the value of diversity of thinking, diversity of upbringing, diversity of background, and diversity of gender. In all my jobs, I have always tried to have one outlier in the group. And it's difficult for the group, because you bring somebody who is, you know, a different color, different gender, different background, different upbringing, different way of thinking, different way of talking. And this, I mean, the group resists. It can be very irritating. Always, yes. But we have to welcome that irritation, because that person is going to bring something different. He will see or she will see the world in a different way, and we need to have that. Absolutely. Independence. You initiated a statement where you gave full support to the American Fed chair. Why is it important for a central bank to be so independent? For two reasons. One is, we have a narrow mandate, and we have to deliver on that mandate, and we have to be accountable. But to do that, we need a level of independence; it is the best word to describe it. In other words, being exclusively focused on the mandate requires that you are not, sort of, vulnerable and moved around and shaken by other imperatives that people have. So to singly focus on that, you are better off not having others insert their own priorities, their own prejudice in what you have to deliver. Singleness of mandate. That's one. The second one is an issue of timing. Because when we decide on the monetary policy front—whether you hike, whether you cut, whether you hold, whether you think that you are at such a lower bound level that you have to use alternative tools—those decisions take time. It doesn't deliver immediately a result. It's a matter of 6, 9, 12, sometimes more months. Whereas political leaders and fiscal authorities have different timing imperatives. They think about two things: the immediate effect in terms of the public opinion, and they follow their ship, or their readership, or their votership, and the next election. That's the timing imperative that they operate under. And there is nothing wrong with that. That's the way politicians operate. But if I tell a politician who says to me, "we need to deliver some good for the public opinion because they are concerned about this or that," I cannot do that, and I shouldn't do that, because this is going to impact in a year's time. How worried have you been about the independence of the US central bank, including also the quality of the data and so on being generated? You know, I don't want to interfere with US domestic policy and pass judgment on the current state of affairs as far as the Fed is concerned. What I was concerned about was the way in which somebody whom many central bankers around the world regard as competent, independent, a beacon of integrity, was being challenged. And we thought that—I thought personally—that we should give him all the support that he deserves. And you know, I don't want to interfere with the political life of the United States, but this is how we perceive him, and this is how we value the independence of our central banks, including the Fed, which is the largest in the world. How is the independence of the ECB different from the Fed? At the ECB we are lucky because independence is engraved in the treaty. And I cannot receive a call from any leader in Europe, or from the president of the commission, asking me to do this, or suggesting that I have not done enough of that, or that I have done too much of this. They cannot do it, and they know that. The rookies don't know, but they find out. Let's spend some time on Europe and what we're seeing here now. So we've had some quite weak numbers in terms of productivity in Europe, including recently in Germany. Just how did we get to this point? This is not a recent phenomenon. It goes back to, you know, I would say that we in Europe have missed the internet revolution. And when you trace back the evolution of productivity between Europe and the United States, it's pretty obvious that this is where it started diverging, where the US continued to increase productivity, not at a very fast pace, but increased, whereas we remained stable and low, and we never caught up with that. The real question we need to ask ourselves now is, is the use of artificial intelligence, not in a pioneering way as the US does because there is a very strong concentration of data, capital, appetite, and probably expertise that is in some parts of the United States, but the real question for us is, will the diffusion and use of that help us move our productivity up? We're beginning to see some of that; productivity numbers are beginning to shake up. We'll see. Do you think we realistically can compete with the US in AI? Not in the pioneering phase. Whether you look at chips manufacturing and sophistication, whether you look at accumulation of data, whether you look at the price of energy, I think the US has a very significant advantage. But the pioneering is one thing, the diffusion is another one. And when we look at the diffusion even at SME levels, Europe is not lagging behind. Europe is actually a little bit ahead of the US in terms of penetration of AI in the manufacturing process and the services elaboration. Europe is not lagging behind at the moment. When do you think we could start to see the AI effect on productivity? Hard to say, because this AI creation, diffusion, and penetration is so much faster than anything we've seen before. You know, when you compare electricity and AI—AI new iterations and changes come about in three weeks. Well, maybe I'm exaggerating. Let's say three months. But and diffusion probably a little more than that. Let's even assume that it's three years. The first ChatGPT was about three years ago. If you look at my organization here, everybody has one artificial intelligence agent that he uses, that he or she uses all the time. Not yet in a very sophisticated way for most, although in some segments, yes, very much so. Three years. Electricity, the diffusion of electricity, 30 years. So when do we see the productivity impact? How do we measure it? Probably a lot faster than what we think. How we do that is under review. Will AI be deflationary? I wouldn't say at this point in time. To the extent that it improves significantly productivity, it should. But you know, what are the other factors we need to take into account? Which ones? Energy. Well, you know, to teach an LLM at the pioneering edge where they are now costs an absolute fortune, right? And it consumes masses of energy as well. We're talking about one billion dollars at the moment, close to that. How will that impact prices? How will it be amortized depending on the business model and depending—and we circle back to the beginning of our conversation—fragmentation. Will fragmentation of the geopolitical world as we see it now be conducive to the diffusion of AI and the leveraging of AI on a global basis, which would be necessary for those new LLMs to produce new artificial intelligence tools so that they can be properly amortized over the course of a big scale of users? Big question. Absolutely. There is an argument that the new Fed chair has a deep understanding of what AI will do to productivity, and therefore that they will front-load interest rate cuts because of that. Do you think that argument holds? That takes us back to the Greenspan days, right? I think what we are seeing around the world is going to possibly disrupt even that theory. If the price of energy is reassessed because of the vulnerabilities that we have around shipping transportation and insurance, and the limited push for renewable energies—I think the jury is out. Let's see. The situation is vastly different, and if you look at public debt for instance, we're not in the same situation as in the Greenspan days. We touched on the productivity in Europe, and we had the Draghi report, but not so much has happened. Why is it so difficult to fix this problem? I would first of all contend that some developments have taken place. It's not fast enough, it's not enough of it, but it is taking place. There are simplification programs underway, there is a move towards capital market union, which I have been talking about for such a long time, and which I am beginning to see the developments of. Is it enough? Is it fast enough? No. Why is it difficult? Because of Europe. Because Europe is a club of 27 member states, who in order to avoid the horrible wars that destroyed our continent, decided to bring themselves together with the same objectives. But it is hard because each of them is sovereign, each of them has its territory, its defense, its voting. So you are talking about a lot of territorial aspects which are causing these changes that are necessary to happen slowly, or to be delayed by what I call the gold plating that takes place at the national level. I'll give you an example. You decide through a directive that, I don't know, supervision of financial institutions will take place in such a way. The gold plating happens because each and every national authority in charge of supervision will say, "yeah, we'll do that, but we will also add this, and we will add another little ratio here, a little buffer there," and you end up with 27 different regimes. So I think the objective that the European authorities hopefully will have is: let's implement together with regulation. Regulation you cannot mess around with; you apply the regulation. Do strong labor interests in Europe hold back change? The whole labor market is under the shock of demographic evolution, right? So the working force in most of Europe is beginning to decline, which will require new forces. I think we've exhausted the women forces, although there might be some pockets of availability. We will be employing people for a longer period of their life. So I think we will have debates around pensions, about pension financing, about the age of retirement, and we should face that. And we will have discussions, which will be difficult, about immigration because labor will probably be needed. And for those who hope that AI will substitute everything, I don't think so. Now, will that be a force that sort of withholds development, productivity, progress, and entrepreneurship? I hope not, but it will require some cultural changes because at the moment the concept of vested interest, accrued rights, and delay for anything that is decided is an impediment to the changes that are necessary. But it has to be taken in the context of the entire labor market and the changes that will affect it. But do we also have to rethink the combined power of employee representatives, labor representatives, unions, and that type of strength? Do we need to rethink what it does to the change in Europe? I think given the history we have, those changes will happen if we can embrace everyone, if we include. And you know, I always keep in my mind a conversation I had with one of the Baltic state prime ministers, who went through an IMF program and that process went extremely well and changes took place. And I asked him—I said, what was the recipe and how do you—where do you attribute the success? Now many prime ministers would have said, "oh it's me, you know, I did the job." He was modest enough to say one of the key reasons it happened so well in my country is because from the get-go we included the trade unions. We shared with them the difficulties, the progress we had to make, the changes that had to take place, and the reforms that we would have to take to parliament. And of course it wasn't 100 percent of what we wanted to achieve, but it was 80 percent, and it was 80 percent with the consent and the support of all forces in the nation. I think that's the only way to go. It requires a lot of changes, but I think it's necessary. After COVID, you have said that you were slow to see the inflationary pressure. Have you—a bit slow? I didn't say slow. A bit slow. I would say pretty slow, but pretty slow, a bit slow, whatever. Have you changed the way ECB works so that you would have entered that situation differently now? I think history and past decisions inform the analysis that we do, the decision making process that we will follow, and compared with that period of '22, for instance, I think we have better sensors and better analytical tools in order to really appreciate how soon we have to decide. But there is a huge, huge uncertainty around and a volatility that is just, as I said earlier, I think unprecedented. Did you have somebody at that stage who was just the odd one out who said, "President Lagarde, you are wrong, this inflation is great and we are underestimating it?" I had one, but I did not pay enough attention to that person. Right. And we should collectively have—we were not the only one, by the way, so it's not consolation and it doesn't eliminate the regret that I have to have felt completely bound by previous forward guidance. That's the regret that I have. Why is 2 percent such a magic number in the central bank world? It came from New Zealand of all places, down under. Sure, it was a wild batch. It's stark. Yeah. You know, I think deviation from 2 percent will come after my time. I'm not going to change that. What do you think about the dollar as a reserve currency in the long term? Well, it's currently a reserve currency. It has about 60 percent of central bank reserves, it's about 40 percent of international trade invoicing, and it is, you know, in times of high crisis, it is still a destination for capital flows. There's no doubt about that. Are we seeing the level of confidence slightly undermined by various factors? Probably so, but these things move over the course of time, and I see our job here at the European Central Bank as to defend the euro, to make sure that it's available, and that liquidity is there to facilitate the invoicing and the recourse to euros in terms of crisis. This is why we recently changed the regime of our Euro-Repo line in order to make it available on a much broader basis to national central banks around the world. And I think that our duty is also to make sure that our currency, the euro, is available in digital form, because the world is going digital and I don't see why a central bank currency would not also adopt the digital form. Why is a digital euro so important when it comes to making the euro an important—or continuing to keep the euro as an important currency? If I talk to my children and my grandchildren, do they carry banknotes? Not much. No. Do they pay digitally? Yes, so. Do I think that central bank money is critical in order to structure, organize, and hold the financial system? Yes. So my conclusion from those two elements is we should have a central bank currency which is available in digital form. Added to which, I think that the rail guards on which digital currencies and digital assets travel is a public good, and I think that we should be building it. Does that mean that only the digital euro travels on those rail guards? No. When will we have a digital euro in volume? It will be in a pilot phase, parliamentary decisions voluntarily, in 2027 and then complete rollout in '29. I'm just wondering whether with all the AI agents, which I believe need stablecoins and chains to transact with each other, that we potentially could see a very big uptake here. I'm not convinced of the business case of stablecoins, to be perfectly honest. However, it's not my area of expertise, but I'm just wondering. But I think that there is a market failure which stablecoins have identified, and that is the cross-border payments. So if the financial agents, and in particular the traditional banking sector, can remedy that and the digital euro can be an instrument, then that element of business case goes. And the rest at the moment, if you look at the purpose that they serve, it's essentially to facilitate the transition between the crypto world to the fiat currency world. That's what stablecoins essentially do at the moment. I'm not sure that it's necessary—a single purpose that will be producing real outcomes. And we see it in the numbers. If you look at the progression of stablecoins, for instance, they have significantly increased up until last October. Since last October, it's flat. Do you think quantum computing can break the chains and make these things worthless? I think quantum computing is going to be a significant enhancer of many of the work that we do in the digital world, and I think in particular the capacity to compute with a lower level of energy is going to be the result of quantum computing. Moving on to leadership. How are you as a leader? Oh, you have to ask the other people, not me. But what are your principles? What do you believe in? I believe in listening, in learning, in working, and ultimately in leading as a result. But am I an inclusive leader rather than a sort of "I'm on the top of the hill, follow my flag" type? I'm more of an inclusive leader. I don't take personal pleasure out of, you know—I instruct, I order, I exercise power. This is not something that I find neither satisfying nor particularly productive. So I'm more of an inclusive person, yeah. It's external factors and external political rebalancing and balancing that are taking place outside our institution. Forbes ranked you as the second most powerful woman in the world. Do you reflect on that? Not at all. But the first time it happened, my youngest son said to me, "Only second? Why aren't you first?" And I thought, "Oh, bloody hell, this is not bad." And he said, "Well, now you know how it felt when we brought back a B and you say why not an A." Fair enough. So the one who is number one is Ursula von der Leyen, right? And so if you had her job, what would you have done? Probably not as well as she is doing. What is she doing particularly well? I think she's managing to keep 27 very sizeable egos on the same plane, and that is very hard. Because of that European construction, you have 27 leaders who are in charge of their constituency, accountable to their constituency, and have an electoral timeline. As a result of that—not because of who they are, but as a result of that—they lose sight of the medium to longer term horizon more often than not. Not all of them, and not all the time, but I think her job is to keep an eye on keeping that ship with everybody on board, or as many as possible on board, and looking at the destination and keeping the eye on the destination. That's hard. But you are kind of doing the same thing, right? Within my mandate, within my mandate. And in many ways at that point in my life, I was rescued by that year that I spent in the United States at a time when there was no cellphone, no emails, no fax machine, and you know, in the scholarship that I received, there was very little money available to stay in touch with the family. Property Play: JLL CEO on how global conflicts and capital shifts are reshaping CRE

CNBC Television(3.3M) ·32:16 ·680 views · 2026-03-24
Christian Ulbrich (CEO, JLL) Diana Olick (Real Estate Correspondent, CNBC)
Despite geopolitical volatility and market fears over AI disruption, JLL is seeing a recovery in commercial real estate driven by a "flight to quality" in core assets and significant productivity gains from decade-long investments in proprietary data and AI tools.
TL;DR Despite geopolitical volatility and market fears over AI disruption, JLL is seeing a recovery in commercial real estate driven by a "flight to quality" in core assets and significant productivity gains from decade-long investments in proprietary data and AI tools. ...

TL;DR

Despite geopolitical volatility and market fears over AI disruption, JLL is seeing a recovery in commercial real estate driven by a "flight to quality" in core assets and significant productivity gains from decade-long investments in proprietary data and AI tools.

Geopolitical Volatility and Capital Shifts

  • Geopolitical Impact: Global conflicts, particularly in the Middle East, have interrupted a strong regional growth trajectory and triggered a "normal" short-term reaction in interest rates.
      • JLL remains optimistic for a strong 2026 outlook, noting the global economy entered this period of uncertainty from a position of strength.
  • Shift to Real Assets: Investors are increasingly viewing real estate as a hedge against uncertainty.
      • The "Core" Strategy: Private capital is gravitating toward the most conservative routes—investing in Grade A buildings in prime locations across all asset classes (office, logistics, and multifamily).
      • Investor Tiers: Smaller investors are targeting public REITs, while institutional and ultra-high-net-worth individuals are moving toward direct real estate investments.

Sector Outlook: Office, Multifamily, and Data Centers

  • The "Grade A" Office Premium: There is a severe shortage of high-end office space globally. In markets like New York, the newest sustainable buildings command a 94% rent premium over older inventory.
      • Class B Conversions: Upgrading Class B buildings to Class A status is viable only where rent growth justifies the high cost of construction.
  • Multifamily Weakness: The sector is experiencing "wobbling" due to a surge in new supply and shifting household demographics.
      • Success in multifamily is now highly dependent on specific sub-market locations and clearly defined target demographics (e.g., affluent vs. middle class).
  • Data Center Bottlenecks: While demand for AI-driven data capacity is massive, growth is currently limited by a lack of power grid availability rather than a lack of capital or interest.

AI: From Perceived Threat to Growth Driver

  • Addressing Market Misconceptions: Ulbrich characterizes the recent sell-off of real estate service stocks due to AI fears as "unfair" and a misunderstanding of the business model.
      • Market Share Gains: JLL views itself as an "AI winner," using proprietary tools (like "Blackbird" and "Falcon") to gain market share and increase margins by boosting employee productivity.
  • Workforce Transformation: Rather than mass layoffs, AI is allowing JLL to scale revenue without significantly increasing headcount.
      • The company has already trained 30,000 employees on AI tools to transition them into more insightful, data-driven roles.
  • Data as the "Untouched Treasure": JLL’s competitive advantage stems from 10 years of collecting operational building data, which AI then processes to provide client advice that "monoliner" competitors cannot match.

Sustainability and Urban Evolution

  • Sustainability as a Business Case: Ulbrich argues that sustainability is a fundamental financial imperative, not a political one.
      • Rising energy prices make low-consumption buildings a necessity for corporate bottom lines, regardless of the political climate.
  • The Future of CBDs: Central Business Districts (CBDs) are evolving into "liveable, walkable" hubs that mix office, residential, and entertainment.
      • The AI Effect: In cities like San Francisco, the AI boom is actively bringing tenants back into the city center, proving to be a stronger recovery catalyst than post-COVID return-to-office mandates.

Strategic Advice for Investors

  • Operational Reality: Real estate should no longer be treated like a passive bond investment.
      • Management Intensity: Success requires active operation and data-driven expertise; investors who "buy and go on holiday" risk significant losses in the current environment.
  • Nearshoring Opportunities: Supply chain disruptions and geopolitical turmoil are driving a massive shift toward "nearshoring."
      • This creates a long-term "uptick" opportunity for modern industrial and logistics facilities located closer to home markets.
"We cannot see any significant risk of disruption to our business model. In fact... we are a clear winner of the AI age because we have invested very heavily, early on, ahead of our competitors." — Christian Ulbrich
"Sustainability is not a political question. It's very much a fundamental business question... it makes a lot of sense to place your people into buildings which are sustainable, which have low energy consumption." — Christian Ulbrich
"Investing into real estate, you are investing into something you have to operate... It is a business. And for every business you are investing in, you either should be the expert yourself or you should get yourself an expert, otherwise you will lose money." — Christian Ulbrich

Well, first and foremost I would say that central business districts have always been under the threat to constantly evolve, to change, to adapt. We had multiple incidents and developments in the past which forced central business districts to change. If you go a long term back, it was very hard to access the city centers. And then in the 60s and in the 70s communities decided to kind of cut massive roads into those cities so that people can easily access central business districts by car. How the we use AI in practice | AI Summit 2026 | Norges Bank Investment Management

Norges Bank Investment Management(104.0K) ·57:33 ·4.7K views · 2026-03-24
> "The issue is what we call the technology overhang.
TL;DR: Norges Bank Investment Management (NBIM) is executing an aggressive AI transformation aiming for a 20% efficiency gain and a 50% reduction in manual processes by 2028 through mandatory upskilling, cloud-native data architecture, and the deployment of specialized AI agents...

TL;DR: Norges Bank Investment Management (NBIM) is executing an aggressive AI transformation aiming for a 20% efficiency gain and a 50% reduction in manual processes by 2028 through mandatory upskilling, cloud-native data architecture, and the deployment of specialized AI agents across all fund functions.

The Foundation: Data Infrastructure and Cultural Adoption

  • Technological Overhang: CEO Nicolai Tangen highlighted that the primary challenge is not the technology itself, but the "technology overhang"—the ability of an organization to absorb and utilize nearly vertical growth in tech capabilities.
  • Infrastructure Transformation: COO Birgitte Bryne outlined three foundational steps taken since 2015:
      • In-sourcing Operations: Bringing settlement and valuation in-house to own the data and expertise.
      • Public Cloud Migration: Moving all IT infrastructure to the cloud to achieve "data horizons" and instant scalability.
      • The "NBIM Core" Data Warehouse: A massive data-cleaning initiative to centralize high-quality internal and external data, essential for AI functionality.
  • Mandatory Upskilling: Stian Wisth (Head of AI) emphasized that voluntary training fails those who need it most. NBIM implemented mandatory 30-minute AI training sessions for all staff, supported by an "Ambassador Network" of 20 volunteers solving localized use cases.
  • Agile Evolution: The fund is moving away from traditional 1990s Scrum methodology (large teams, heavy rituals) toward Autonomous Trios (two developers, one business person) to increase development speed using AI tools.

Responsible AI and Governance Framework

  • Risk-Based Approach: Lydia (AI Compliance Officer) detailed a framework aligned with the EU AI Act and global standards. Requirements scale based on the system's impact; for example, email filtering is treated differently than investment-related AI.
  • Human-in-the-Loop: The framework mandates human involvement in all investment and people-related decisions.
  • AI Governance Working Group: A cross-departmental team ensures that "responsible AI" is a daily practice rather than just a compliance checkbox, focusing on data protection and legal alignment.

Investment Intelligence and Forensic Accounting

  • Block Trade Agents: Ole demonstrated a multi-agent system that analyzes massive share sale requests (e.g., a 30bn NOK Ferrari deal) in under an hour. Agents pull data on ownership, market sentiment, and index effects to provide a rapid decision basis.
  • Forensic Accounting: Morten explained how AI identifies "financial makeup" (revenue/cost manipulation) across 7,000 companies by scanning 16 years of footnotes. The model predicts the likelihood of a company becoming a "forensic case" and a subsequent share price collapse.
  • ESG Screening: Kristine (London) highlighted that screening 7,000 companies for risks like forced labor would normally require 3,000 analysts. A two-phase AI system now flags high-risk companies for the fund’s eight-person ESG team to review.

Operational Efficiency and Risk Mitigation

  • Cybersecurity "Tripwires": Johan described an agent that monitors a trillion data points for suspicious activity. It performs initial investigations in five minutes that previously took a human 30 minutes, producing a full triage report while the analyst is still "rolling out of bed."
  • Financial Reporting: Torjus noted that AI (using Claude and Cursor) has automated the production of IFRS-compliant notes. One specific report (Note 14) that used to take one person a full week is now completed in two hours.
  • Market Impact Reduction: Jon Egil detailed how AI predicts market patterns to reduce the fund's "footprint." By using AI to determine when to be patient with trades or "park" orders internally, NBIM saved an estimated 4–6 billion NOK in 2025.
  • Negotiation Simulation: Christy (Legal/Tax) uses a negotiation simulator that predicts 80% of counterparty arguments and allows staff to practice live negotiations in "voice mode" to secure better contract terms.

Notable Quotes

"Has anybody thanked you for cleaning your data? No... You basically tell them that 31st of January, we are going to turn the old data off. And if you sit there the day after and have no data, you are going to look very stupid." — Nicolai Tangen and Birgitte Bryne (COO)
"Can it [AI training] be voluntary? No, because the people who don't want to do it are the people who need it the most. It has to be mandatory and you have to be on them like a wasp." — Nicolai Tangen, CEO
"We are now in a place where we ourselves become our own worst enemy in the market. When we buy, we buy so much we push prices up... AI is helping us as human beings to understand more, faster, deeper." — Jon Egil, NBIM

Goldman is reaching out to a few big investors and wants to know if we're in, by how much, and at what price. They need an answer within one hour. Would you buy? In our team, we get about 200 of these requests every year. With time, these transactions have contributed billions to the fund's excess returns. We clearly see that the better we are at using data to decide when to pass, when to participate, and when to really swing big, the more money we make. These transactions come with risks, and every deal is different. To decide on the Ferrari deal, there's a lot of things we need to understand, like who's selling and why? Was it anticipated by the market? How have similar things gone in the past? What is a fair price? Will this trigger forced buying by index trackers? And so on. But the challenge is that we have very little time and the data is everywhere. It's in external and internal sources. It's in text and numbers. It's in databases, web searches, and algorithms. The output of one might affect the output of another, which makes it very difficult to automate because you cannot solve everything by code and you cannot solve everything by a language model. We need both. So we built agents: specialized AI programs with dedicated tasks and tools working together. To give an example, here you can see one agent searching the web to find out who's really the owner behind this holding company. Another agent takes the deal text and pulls out the most important data points, and it's all sent through to a third agent which runs an algorithm to calculate whether this will trigger an index effect or not. In reality, there are more agents with more tools. But the point is that in very short time, we have the full decision basis ready with more data and better analysis than before. We started this by building a prototype within the investment team before we got help from a very talented developer, Johan, who is here. He helped us take what we started and build something real. So when Goldman calls, we spend less time gathering all data and more time analyzing it, which leads to better decisions and means that we make more money. Thank you. So we're moving on to communication. Transparency is a big thing for the fund, probably the most transparent fund there is. What's cool about our communication department, as well as being good communicators, is that it's become a lot more data-driven. To explain a use case, Sara will take us through. Echo is a live overview of all of our communications activities across channels. We're not developers, but we built this ourselves using AI tools. This last year, we've been working on taking this from providing stats to actual insights. In 2025, the fund was mentioned in almost 50,000 articles. So far this year, more than 5,000. For a press team of only two people, it's simply impossible to keep track of everything that's being written. That's why we built an AI-powered sentiment analysis to help us do exactly that. This is an agent-based system where each article goes through a main agent that delegates to specialized sub-agents. They classify the article's sentiment, our participation in the article, the priority of the media outlet, the article type, and how prominent the fund is in the article, as well as topics and people being mentioned. All of this data is stored directly into our data warehouse, Snowflake. Existing media monitoring tools are expensive and honestly not that good. Building this ourselves is cheaper, and we're able to display the data exactly the way we want it. Here you can see one of the sentiment pages that we built in Echo. This is a little while back and as you can see, there was quite a bit of negative coverage at the time. We built a timeline where you can easily drill down to see exactly who has written what. We've also built an insights feature where we're using AI to summarize the coverage and provide us with the key highlights. This enables us to faster get an overview of everything that's being written, what's driving the coverage, and where we might need to act. Lastly, we built a chatbot sitting on top of Echo being an expert on all of the comms data. Instead of digging through the dashboard, we can simply ask something like "analyze engagement on social media". Echobot then dives into Snowflake, fetches data on LinkedIn, Instagram, and YouTube, and generates a report. What's important here is that this is not a predefined view. It analyzes across channels on the spot. It can identify trends and come up with strategic recommendations. Previously, this would require us to log in to each of these platforms, fetch the data, and put this together ourselves. We've essentially automated some of our internal reporting. AI has enabled us to build our own systems, automate analysis, and make better decisions faster. Essentially, when you run a fund like this, you are risk managing. You've seen as of late, markets up and down, energy prices up and down. That is something that we can stomach; that is actually something that we can play to our advantage. If there's one risk that potentially keeps us awake at night, it's cybersecurity risk. Johan, how do you keep us in bed? We'll see. Part of what I do working with cybersecurity in the fund is that I think about: how will it look if somebody is trying to attack us? How will it look if somebody is trying to steal money and basically defraud us? Part of this is that I maintain, together with my colleagues, basically a giant network of invisible tripwires across all of our digital infrastructure. This is quite a big sort of data collection operation. Just to give you a sense of scale, we collect roughly a trillion data points about NBIM and NBIM's operation throughout the year. From those trillion, we surface down maybe somewhere in the range of a million to 100,000 things that might be considered suspicious. Then we have to take a broad stroke on that and surface only a very, very tiny portion that we believe is of high value down to myself and my colleagues. To give you an example of what something like that might look like: an employee who likes football might be using their computer to stream a football match. They might have browsed to some shady place on the internet and gone to a website that we have intelligence on that says this website also does bad stuff. Then what might happen is that we might get an alert. I might, for example, be woken in the middle of the night and I have to figure out what's going on. I will usually then just get an alert that says "this computer connected to a bad place on the internet". Gathering all that context and gathering all the surrounding information from the trillion data points that tells me this is just a normal user that searched for something on Google—constructing that full story is a human judgment task. I have to decide in what direction do I go and what do I look at, what's important and what confirms my hypothesis that this is benign, or what is an indication that this might be bad. When I get this phone call in the middle of the night, an agent starts at the exact same time. While I'm rolling out of bed, this agent has started working immediately. It does the exact same process. It's trying to figure out where do I need to look, what data do I need to pull in, and what's important. It's making all these small judgment calls and ultimately it produces a report or an investigation which is very similar to what I would do that I can use for my initial triage. It does this quite well. It's gotten really, really good over the last year. To put it into perspective, I would say that it does what would have taken me roughly half an hour in five minutes. The other very nice part is, of course, that it's never lazy. Stuff that might be repeating and be very similar, it does with the same amount of work every single time. Thank you. Thanks, Johan. The fund has millions and millions of transactions in more than 60 markets every year. These are highly regulated markets, so you want to make sure that all of these trades that we do are legit and comply with market regulations. Oscar, how do you use technology to improve on that? The risk is real. Cases of insider trading and market manipulation are not rare. We read about them often. We're also seeing active enforcement in the Nordics. This is a big deal. Market integrity is vital for any market participant, and for an investor like NBIM, documenting proper compliance is foundational. How did we get here? Well, with increasing focus on stricter regulation, the buy-side, like NBIM, was now required to demonstrate own trade monitoring capabilities, traditionally relying on the sell-side banks to cover this for us. NBIM selected an external system in 2018 that uses advanced market risk models and spits out alerts that the compliance team can manually investigate. But the system doesn't know NBIM. They specialize in trade context, index rebalancing, company news, industry news, timing patterns, and company interactions. She's always on and completes a full audit trail for every case assessed. Every alert is assessed simultaneously, and she's an expert on pattern recognition. There are always three cases where our master agent hands over to a human: when the case is genuinely ambiguous, when you can't automate judgment, and lastly, when a decision has to be owned by a human. That's where we come in and those cases get sent to compliance. We have the same team but with vastly better coverage. Thank you. The starting point for our management of the fund is a benchmark that is given to us by the Ministry of Finance. That benchmark contains roughly 7,000 companies. If we were to implement that benchmark, we would buy an equal share of roughly 1.5% of each of those companies. The question is: do you actually want to own all of those companies? Morten, do you think so? Probably not. In forensic accounting, we have a little bit of a challenge. The challenge is to remove the bad apples from the fund. On average, each analyst spends about two weeks doing a deep dive on each of these companies. We look through the financial statements and the footnotes. There are millions of pages to read in all the 7,000 companies that we look to sell from. What we need to do is to remove the financial makeup that makes these companies look better than they really are. We need to clean up the accounts, so to speak, 16 years back in time for all of these 7,000 companies and then we need to train the machines to learn to spot this type of financial makeup. How do we do this? One practical example here is that we're looking for different keywords. So here's "payables extension"; this is what we're looking for. We have found the keyword here in the footnotes. Then AI is picking out relevant sentences in those couple of pages before and after. Then it picks out the number we're interested in. In this case, a donut producer has extended her payables by 745 million dollars. We store that and then we learn from it. We're making many different kinds of agents that are trying to spot this kind of financial makeup. This is the companies moving revenues and costs, earnings, or cash flow back or forth in time to look better than they really are. The changes can be quite substantial for some of these adjustments. Then we're having a machine learning model learn from all this data. What we have made in-house is a unique data set where we have gone through every forensic accounting research shop and gotten all of their historical cases where a company has put makeup on their accounts. When the market found out, the share price fell quite a lot. We're having thousands of these cases and we're training the machine learning model to spot similar cases. The output of the model is the percentage likelihood that a company is going to develop into such a forensic accounting case and the share price is going to fall. This model is in production and it is used daily in cooperation with Ole Syrstad and his team. This time and effort came at a cost. We spent so much time in production mode that little was left for deeper analysis and insights. Responsible investing is a big thing for the fund, right? We have deployed new technology also to improve our risk monitoring when it comes to E, S, and G factors. Hello everyone. With AI, we can screen companies at a scale we simply couldn't before. The concept is simple: for each company name, the AI searches across any publicly available source—think news, financial data, government records, and local media—and outputs a structured risk assessment. The key to making a system like this really valuable for us is that our expertise is involved in every step of the process. The screening process runs in two phases. For the first phase, we use a lighter and faster AI model. Here the AI looks for any indication of a company's involvement in the issues we screen for. As expected, most companies clear this stage. But when anything comes up, it triggers phase two. In phase two, we deploy multiple AI agents, each responsible for researching the company from a different angle. This means that one agent could be tracing supply chain links, another agent could be looking at direct operations, while a third agent looks at financial relationships. Once all of our agents are done working, they come together, summarize their findings, and give each company a risk score. For step two, our human analysts jump back in and make the decisions. We review every company that the AI flagged as high risk. This means that us humans, we verify the sources, we check the reasoning, and we make the final decision. If the risk is confirmed, the high-risk company is flagged in two of our internal portfolio management systems: Polaris and in our investment simulator. In addition to being flagged, we can also choose to engage with company management or, in the most serious cases for the small companies in our portfolio, we can choose to do a risk-based divestment. Today we can use AI to screen many more companies so that our team can focus on what actually matters. We can of course not promise that we catch everything. But what we can say is that we can screen more companies, look at more sources, more languages, and go more in-depth than ever before. So that when we see a risk, we can act. Biology's Waymo Moment: Ginkgo Bioworks' Jason Kelly

Sequoia Capital(133.0K) ·58:28 ·507 views · 2026-03-24
Jason Kelly (Founder, Ginkgo Bioworks)
The most significant bottleneck in science is manual labor, not intelligence; by integrating AI reasoning models with autonomous robotic labs, Ginkgo Bioworks has demonstrated that the cost of discovery can be collapsed and the speed of breakthrough ...
TL;DR The most significant bottleneck in science is manual labor, not intelligence; by integrating AI reasoning models with autonomous robotic labs, Ginkgo Bioworks has demonstrated that the cost of discovery can be collapsed and the speed of breakthrough accelerated by treating...

TL;DR

The most significant bottleneck in science is manual labor, not intelligence; by integrating AI reasoning models with autonomous robotic labs, Ginkgo Bioworks has demonstrated that the cost of discovery can be collapsed and the speed of breakthrough accelerated by treating experimental science as an engineering problem rather than a manual craft.

The "Waymo Moment" for Laboratories

  • The Autonomy Analogy: Jason Kelly describes a paradigm shift in lab work comparable to transportation.
      • Traditional Automation (The Subway): High automation but zero flexibility; it only works for repetitive, fixed tasks like diagnostic screening.
      • Manual Labor (The Car): High flexibility but zero automation; 99% of lab work currently involves PhDs moving liquids by hand at a bench.
      • Autonomous Labs (The Waymo): AI enables "high-mix, low-volume" work, providing the flexibility of a human scientist with the 24/7 efficiency of a robot.
  • Engineering vs. Science: Ginkgo has pivoted to solving the Engineering Problem (how to make experiments cheaper and faster) rather than just the Science Problem (designing the perfect cell), believing that massive data generation will eventually render scientific challenges "to dust."
  • Technological Hurdles: Achieving autonomy requires solving two "tractable" problems:
      • Integration: Connecting thousands of long-tail, third-party lab tools into a unified software-controlled stack.
      • Liquid Handling: Automating the "expert thumb" required to manage different liquid viscosities (syrups vs. waters) using AI-vision and feedback loops.

AI Reasoning in Experimental Science

  • OpenAI Landmark Results: In a joint project, Ginkgo used an OpenAI reasoning model to optimize self-free protein synthesis.
      • Performance: The model beat the state-of-the-art benchmark by 40% after only six rounds of experimentation.
      • The "Unfair Advantage": The AI didn’t win by being "smarter" than humans, but by running 100 parallel hypotheses simultaneously, sharing raw data across all "AI scientists" daily, and operating without the constraints of human social hierarchies or publication delays.
  • Logic over Simulation: Kelly argues that AI doesn't need to perfectly simulate biology to move the needle; it only needs the logical reasoning to design an experiment, interpret the data, and "poke" the physical world again.
  • The UX of Science: Ginkgo is moving toward a "Natural Language to Protocol" interface. Instead of scientists coding in Python or using clunky visual tools, they submit written protocols that a model converts into robotic instructions.

Economics and the "Cloud Lab" Model

  • Flipping the Cost Structure: Currently, less than 5% of research budgets go toward reagents (the actual chemicals of discovery). The remaining 95% is "overhead"—manual labor, underutilized real estate, and duplicated equipment.
      • Utilization Gains: Automated labs can move equipment utilization from sub-20% (manual bench) to 70%+.
      • Data per Dollar: By shifting the dominant cost to reagents, Kelly predicts a 10x to 100x increase in the amount of data generated per research dollar.
  • The "Local Cloud" Concept: Future labs will not be distributed across every university floor but will be centralized, dense, robotic hubs.
      • Case Study: Ginkgo recently sold 97 robots to the Department of Energy for "Project Genesis," an initiative aimed at doubling the pace of American science.
      • Accessibility: Ginkgo launched a service where an experiment can cost as little as $39, allowing anyone to submit a query and receive molecular data back without ever touching a pipette.

National Competitiveness and Future Applications

  • The China Challenge: Kelly highlights a major shift in biopharma innovation. Three years ago, China accounted for <5% of new drug discovery deals; last quarter, they reached 40%+.
      • Labor Arbitrage: China’s advantage currently stems from having more scientists who are paid less to do manual lab work. Kelly argues the U.S. must automate to regain a competitive edge.
  • From Disease to Wellness: The "therapeutic" model is limited by regulatory timelines. Kelly sees the next "iPhone-level" breakthroughs in consumer-facing biology:
      • GLP-1s and Beyond: Drugs like Ozempic represent the transition of biotech into "consumer willpower" and lifestyle enhancement.
      • Molecular Monitoring: Moving beyond "pathetic" Oura ring metrics (heart rate) to weekly molecular blood readouts to track aging and interventions in real-time.
  • Democratizing Discovery: Just as the 1960s mainframe era gave way to personal computing, Kelly envisions a future where "millions of people" become scientists by using AI to navigate the esoteric complexities of the lab.

***

Notable Quotes

"All of the previous revolutions in tech—internet, social media, whatever—have been totally meaningless to biotechnology. This is actually going to change the fundamentals of how we do science." — Jason Kelly
"The biggest inefficiency in science isn't intelligence; it's manual labor. 95% of science is five years of moving liquids around the lab bench by hand. It’s an absurdity." — Jason Kelly
"I think Phase 1 is becoming human-level intelligent across a bunch of categories... Phase 2 is going to be becoming super-intelligent across specific categories one at a time. That’s where all the breakthroughs are going to come from." — Pat Grady
"If you 10x or 100x the speed we do scientific discovery, you 10x or 100x the progress of the species." — Jason Kelly

And so the question was: could a reasoning model do the work of experimental science if you gave it a robotic lab? That was the question. And the answer was yeah, it's actually pretty damn good. So basically the way the project worked was we had a biochemistry problem called cell-free synthesis. So you take a piece of DNA, ATCGGG, right? If you were to put it in your cell right now—remember like central dogma in high school, right?—DNA makes RNA makes a protein, right? And so you put that DNA into a cell and it'll make a protein. Well, you can do a thing called cell-free where you pop a cell open, take the guts, put it in a test tube, and then add the DNA to that. And because the guts are still there, it makes the protein. So this is kind of like the world's smallest 3D printer or something, right? Okay. And so scientists use this, they try to optimize it. It's very expensive usually. And so there was a paper that came out of Stanford from Mike Jewett's lab in August that set the benchmark for like how cheap people had been able to do cell-free protein synthesis. And so we said, "Alright, let's try to optimize that with the model." And so we gave the model... we did each round we would do 100 384-well plates. Okay, so each well in a plate is like a little kind of cup of liquid and you can do an experiment in there. And so we gave it, you know, 30,000 experiments to run. And after it would run those experiments, it gets the data back and designs another set. So after four rounds of that we beat the state-of-the-art, and after six rounds we beat it by 40%. Wow. And so that was a... I think it's the most interesting sort of model doing experimental work result that's been shown to date by a lot in my view. And the 40% was a function of what? Just faster cycle time or more intelligent experiment design? Yeah, like how'd it beat the state of the art? So this is my point.
