Anduril Begins Making Uncrewed Fighters

Bloomberg Technology(719.0K) ·13:57 ·1.5K views · 2026-03-24
Trae Stephens (Executive Chairman, Anduril) Caroline Hyde (Host, Bloomberg Technology) Ed Ludlow (Host, Bloomberg Technology)
Anduril has officially commenced operations at its 800,000-square-foot "Arsenal 1" facility in Ohio, shifting toward the mass production of the "Fury" autonomous fighter jet to address the cost asymmetries of modern drone warfare.
TL;DR Anduril has officially commenced operations at its 800,000-square-foot "Arsenal 1" facility in Ohio, shifting toward the mass production of the "Fury" autonomous fighter jet to address the cost asymmetries of modern drone warfare. Executive Chairman Trae Stephens is...

TL;DR

Anduril has officially commenced operations at its 800,000-square-foot "Arsenal 1" facility in Ohio, shifting toward the mass production of the "Fury" autonomous fighter jet to address the cost asymmetries of modern drone warfare. Executive Chairman Trae Stephens is calling for clearer federal policy and a national "re-industrialization" to rebuild U.S. manufacturing capacity and secure critical supply chains.

Manufacturing and Production Scaling

  • "Arsenal 1" Activation: Anduril’s Columbus, Ohio facility is now fully operational. The site is dedicated to the mass production of autonomous systems.
  • Fury Collaborative Combat Aircraft (CCA): The company is beginning production on the "Fury," an autonomous fighter plane.
      • Production Targets: The facility is designed to reach a run rate of approximately 120 to 150 aircraft annually.
  • Capital Allocation: While reports suggest a $4 billion fundraise at a $61 billion valuation, Stephens characterized the current phase as "allocating" high inbound demand rather than a traditional fundraising round.

Software-Defined Warfare and AI

  • The Cost Asymmetry Problem: Stephens highlighted the economic imbalance in modern conflict (e.g., the Middle East), where adversaries use drones costing tens of thousands of dollars, while the U.S. often counters with interceptors costing millions.
  • Lattice AI Operating System: Anduril utilizes "Lattice" as a command-and-control software layer to manage complex airspaces.
      • Kill Chain Analysis: The AI performs real-time analysis to determine the most cost-effective countermeasure for a specific threat, whether via electronic warfare, low-cost munitions, or traditional ballistic interceptors.
  • Autonomous Precedents: Stephens noted that the Department of Defense is already experienced in autonomous systems, citing Policy Directive 3000.09 and the long-standing use of the CIWS (Close-In Weapons System) on naval vessels.

Policy, Ethics, and Governance

  • Rejection of "Philosopher Kings": Stephens argued that tech founders should not be the ones making ethical or policy decisions regarding the use of force. He believes these decisions must remain with elected representatives in a democracy.
  • Call for Regulation: Stephens pushed back against the narrative that tech companies avoid regulation, stating that clear "guardrails" provide the necessary constraints and incentive structures for commercial innovation.
  • Government Abdication: He criticized the "reticence" of Congress and the executive branch to provide clear policy guidelines, labeling it an "abdication of duty" that leaves companies in a state of uncertainty.

Re-Industrialization and Global Investment

  • The New "Arsenal of Democracy": There is a cultural shift within the Pentagon toward streamlining procurement and welcoming new tech entrants to break the traditional monopoly of legacy defense contractors.
  • Industrial Capacity: Beyond AI, Stephens emphasized the urgent need for "re-industrialization." He noted that the U.S. has offshored too much industrial capacity over the last 30 years and needs to rebuild infrastructure in critical sectors like semiconductors.
  • Middle Eastern Capital: Despite regional conflicts, Stephens expects Middle Eastern sovereign wealth funds (KSA, UAE, Qatar) to remain committed investors in U.S. tech and infrastructure as they seek to diversify their economies beyond energy.
"The historic way of doing this... is you have fighter planes, you have bombers. These are very expensive assets... But in modern combat we're seeing hundreds of assets in an airspace... and you have to figure out a way to counter all those systems in a cost-effective way by threat." — Trae Stephens
"I don't want me or Palmer [Luckey] or Brian [Schimpf]... any of my co-founders, making decisions about US policy as it pertains to the use of our capabilities. At the end of the day we are selling to the United States people... and those people have elected representatives." — Trae Stephens
"There is this false narrative that exists that tech companies don't want regulation. I think we don't want excessive regulation, but some regulation is good because it provides us with the guardrails." — Trae Stephens
It was a couple of weeks ago actually that we spoke to you on the show in San Francisco, and at that point it was the early days of the war in Iran. We were trying to make sense of the strategy from the US side, the use of drone technology, and the use of different ballistic missiles. I think probably the best place to start is an update, I guess, from Anduril's side. There aren't any specific reports of where Anduril's technology has or hasn't been used, but you do have anti-drone technology, for example, in deployment at US facilities in the Gulf. That's part of what we'll be talking about here today at Hill and Valley. Yeah, it is true that I mean we have a lot of kit deployed in the region. We've been actively engaged in the conflict. Not a whole lot of details I can provide outside of that of course, but I think there is a big story that has emerged around the asymmetry in drone warfare that's taking place with Iran. They are lobbing drones that cost tens of thousands of dollars, and we're countering them with capabilities that are upwards of several millions of dollars per shot. So one of the goals that Anduril has had since the very beginning, when we started the company in 2017, is to significantly lower the cost of engagement. That means using low-cost munitions and using low-cost interceptors. And that is much of what we have deployed in the region today. I find this point fascinating. We were talking about JDAMs and the Lucas system, the kind of equivalent of what Iran was using. But with time having some distance from Iran, literally distance you know, longer range ballistic missiles will be a part of the story. How—what have you learned about the battlefield present day and where is software coming into it? Because actually that's a big part of what Anduril is working on too. Yeah, I mean you think about the software layer as the command and control for doing air combat essentially. And the historic way of doing this in a Cold War era conflict is you have dozens of assets in an airspace. You know, you might have fighter planes; you might have bombers. These are very expensive assets. And so shooting a two and a quarter million dollar Patriot missile at a MiG-29, that's a pretty good trade. But in modern combat we're seeing hundreds of assets in an airspace: low-cost drones, low-cost cruise missiles, and rockets with a more ballistic trajectory. That is a lot of what Hezbollah has been launching into Iran for decades at this point. And you have to figure out a way to counter all those systems in a cost-effective way by threat. You have to be able to do that analysis to say: is this a thing that we can take out with a ballistic trajectory rocket? Is this something that we have to take out with a Patriot? Or is this something we can take out with electronic warfare? Sorting all of that is, at the end of the day, it's an AI problem. And so, Anduril has this AI operating system backend that we call Lattice. It is used in all of these environments to not only detect and identify and track those assets, but also go through the kill chain analysis and figure out how we can combat these threats in the most cost-effective way possible. AI and warfare is something that's being stress-tested right now in deployment but also from an ethical perspective. You just made a speech here at the Hill and Valley Forum and you talked about philosopher kings—the worry that founders such as yourself start getting involved in the ethical decision-making of the Pentagon. We of course are today expecting a courtroom and administrative perspective of what's happening between Anthropic and the Pentagon. So it feels as though you think Anthropic's gone too far. Well, you know I think that I believe in democracy. And you know, I don't want me or Palmer or Brian or Matt, any of my co-founders, making decisions about US policy as it pertains to the use of our capabilities. At the end of the day, we are selling to the United States people, not just the Department of War. And those people have elected representatives that are making decisions about how these things are used. The 20-year journey to fully autonomous cars with Dmitri Dolgov of Waymo

Stripe(94.0K) ·62:33 ·5.6K views · 2026-03-24
Dmitri Dolgov (Co-CEO, Waymo)
Waymo has transitioned from a 20-year scientific research phase to a massive global scaling operation, now delivering 500,000 autonomous rides weekly by utilizing a unified AI backbone that distills massive off-board foundation models into specialized on-board "drivers.
TL;DR Waymo has transitioned from a 20-year scientific research phase to a massive global scaling operation, now delivering 500,000 autonomous rides weekly by utilizing a unified AI backbone that distills massive off-board foundation models into specialized on-board "drivers."...

TL;DR

Waymo has transitioned from a 20-year scientific research phase to a massive global scaling operation, now delivering 500,000 autonomous rides weekly by utilizing a unified AI backbone that distills massive off-board foundation models into specialized on-board "drivers."

The Technical Stack: Multi-Modal Sensing and AI Distillation

Waymo’s architecture relies on a "Teacher-Student" model where massive off-board foundation models are specialized and then distilled for real-time inference.

  • The Sensor Trinity: The system utilizes three complementary sensing modalities to achieve 360-degree coverage:
      • Lidar (Lasers): Provides high-resolution 3D structural mapping by firing millions of pulses per second.
      • Radar: Offers lower resolution but maintains performance in adverse weather (fog, snow) where cameras fail.
      • Cameras: Provide essential semantic data but are susceptible to lighting conditions and glare.
  • The Trinity of Models: The foundation model is specialized into three "Teachers":
      • The Waymo Driver: The real-time system navigating the vehicle.
      • The Simulator: A generative environment used for closed-loop training and evaluation.
      • The Critic: An opinionated model that identifies "interesting" events and defines "good" vs. "bad" driving behavior for the reward function.
  • Augmented End-to-End Learning: While the system can propagate gradients from pixels to trajectories, Waymo avoids a "pure" black-box approach.
      • Intermediate Representations: The AI is augmented with structured concepts (lanes, signs, objects) to allow for more efficient simulation and safety validation.

Evolution of the "Driver": From Subsystems to AI Backbone

Dmitri Dolgov outlines a major shift in how the software is structured, moving from modular engineering to deep learning.

  • The Discontinuous Jump (Gen 4 to Gen 5):
      • Generation 4: Relied on many small, localized ML subsystems.
      • Generation 5 (Current): Established AI as the primary backbone, enabling the system to generalize across diverse cities (e.g., San Francisco vs. Phoenix) and weather conditions.
  • Emergent Intelligence: The shift to a unified AI backbone has led to "magical" behaviors.
      • Example: A Waymo vehicle detected a pedestrian hidden behind a bus by processing noisy lidar reflections that bounced *under* the vehicle—a feat impossible for human vision or simple geometric models.
  • The Autonomy Gap: Dolgov argues that Level 2/3 driver-assist systems will not naturally evolve into Level 4/5 autonomy. He views them as qualitatively different problems requiring different foundational architectures.

Scaling Operations and the Sixth Generation Vehicle

Waymo is moving away from retrofitted consumer vehicles toward purpose-built autonomous platforms.

  • The 6th Gen Platform: The upcoming custom-designed vehicle (developed with Zeekr/OHAI) is passenger-centric rather than driver-centric.
      • Design: Features sliding doors, a flat floor, and a "living room" feel with significant internal space despite a footprint similar to a standard SUV.
      • Cost Efficiency: The sensor suite is simplified and significantly cheaper—comparable to the cost of high-end driver-assist systems—while being more capable than the 5th Gen hardware.
  • Global Generalization: Waymo is currently expanding to London and Tokyo. While "driving on the left" is easy for the AI to learn, the primary hurdles remain hardware specialization for cold weather (heating elements, cleaning solutions) and regional validation.
  • Operational Automation: The "village" required to run Waymo is becoming automated. Depots now feature an "orchestrated dance" where cars autonomously return for cleaning, flag their own maintenance needs via emoji displays on the roof, and park themselves for charging.

Second-Order Effects: The Future of Cities

The transition to majority-autonomous traffic will fundamentally reshape urban infrastructure and economic models.

  • Traffic "Standing Waves": Autonomous vehicles act as "pro-social" drivers. By eliminating abrupt braking and erratic merging, they can prevent the "standing wave" traffic jams caused by human error.
  • Reclaiming Urban Space: Since autonomous ride-hailing vehicles do not need to sit idle 90% of the day, vast amounts of land currently dedicated to parking lots and garages can be repurposed for housing or public spaces.
  • Commercial Modality: While ride-hailing is the current focus due to density requirements, Dolgov expects the technology to eventually move into personally owned vehicles for rural areas where fleet density is not economically viable.

***

Notable Quotes

"It’s very easy to get started. It’s deceptively easy to get started. But it is super hard to go the full distance and get that... number of nines. Every next nine takes 10x more effort." — Dmitri Dolgov
"I don’t believe [driver-assist and full autonomy] will converge. I see it as fundamentally two different problems... It is a qualitative jump." — Dmitri Dolgov
"What generalizes least well? We’re finding... cold winter weather. You need the hardware, the cleaning solutions, heating elements... You don't get that for free from just pulling in some VLM decoder." — Dmitri Dolgov
"The car did something... our peripheral lidars bounced under the bus and there was just a little bit of very, very noisy reflection of the movement of the person's feet that was enough for the AI models to say, 'Hey, likely there's a pedestrian there.' It blew my mind." — Dmitri Dolgov
Dmitri Dolgov is Co-CEO of Waymo. He joined Google’s self-driving car project in 2009 as one of its first engineers, and was repeatedly promoted until he took it over in 2021. Waymo is Google's most successful moonshot, and now provides over 500,000 fully autonomous rides each week. Cheers, by the way. Yeah, cheers. You grew up in Russia, right? I grew up in Russia. Then I—it was actually the Soviet Union. Right, right, exactly. My dad is a physicist. So, the Soviet Union started falling apart, and then he got a position—a visiting position in a university—in Kyoto University for a year. We moved there as a family, and then he went to Berkeley and I kind of tagged along. And then I graduated from high school. I was thinking about the next thing I wanted to do, and I really liked that technical school in Russia. The Russians are serious about the physics. They are, they are. So I went back to Russia and I got my bachelor's and master's there. What year was this that you went back to Russia? 1994. Okay, so that was kind of almost peak Russian optimism in a sense. It was. It was, yeah, yeah. No, I actually remember talking to my mom about it. Of course, my parents grew up in the Soviet Union. They’ve seen it—they were born right before the war, and then they saw and lived through some really tough times. And I remember talking to my mom. She insisted that I get my green card here in the US before I went back. At that time, I wasn't thinking of coming back. But you know, I was pretty excited about where Russia was on the trajectory it was on. Being young and naive, I was like, "There's no turning back." And so, why did you decide to come back? The school—it was pretty clear to me. I wanted to continue studying math and computer science. While the undergrad and master's that I got in physics and applied math was an incredibly strong foundational school of Russian math and science, for graduate school, it was very clear to me that the best way to do it was in the US. So I came back. I'm struck by how the founders of the two most valuable UK companies are Russian math nerds who both went to the same school—Nikolai Storonsky at Revolut and Alex Gerko at XTX. It's a strong diaspora. There’s a company not far from here where one of the founders also has a similar pedigree. A company that we're closely affiliated with. Exactly. You know the classic engineering interview question: what happens when I type google.com and hit enter? Talk me through whatever you like—HTTP, DNS—and you can go down to whatever level of the stack you want. Do you want to describe, when I take a ride in a Waymo today, what's happening at a technical level? What is the architecture? Let me answer your question about what's happening in real-time, but this is going to be only a part of the story. We're going to be talking about the real-time inference part of it. If we want to have a deeper, richer technical conversation, it would be interesting also to zoom out and talk about the entire ecosystem of what goes into building, evaluating, and deploying the Waymo driver. When you're driving around, or being driven around, we think about what we're building as a driver, obviously not a car. It has a number of sensors that are positioned around the vehicle. We use three different sensing modalities: there's cameras, there's lidars, or lasers, and there are radars. Those are sort of the primary ones. There are also directional microphone arrays, but those are the primary three for sensing the world. They all have very nicely complementary physical properties. They all have 360-degree coverage around the vehicle, so the Waymo driver sees 360 all the time. All of that data goes into a computer and the software that processes it. Now it's all AI—specialized AI in the physical world. It processes the sensor data. Nowadays, you know, we talk about it using AI terminology as encoders that take this data in, and then there's the decoder, the action, the generative part, if you will, in the car. The generative task there is to figure out how to drive, right? And that is, of course, connected through a specialized interface to the car where we can actually actuate the vehicle. That's why you see the steering wheel turn and it drives you around. Okay, so I get into my car, there's three main families of sensors—lidar, radar and cameras. It is using that to first build a model of what's going on in the world—where all the other cars are and things like that—and then, as you say, make decisions and actuate that with the car. That is the system that you're living in. Is all that inference done locally or, presumably yes, nothing's in the cloud? Nothing real-time in the cloud. There are some things that can happen in the cloud, but they're not required. Got it. What's an example of a nice-to-have that happens in the cloud? You can imagine a situation not directly related to the task of driving. Say, after you leave the car, we want to check that the car is not dirty or you didn't leave anything there. If you left a mess, then we want to send the car to one of our depots to get it cleaned up. If you left an item there—your phone or something—we want to detect that and then send it to our lost and found and let you know. So we do that by asking a model that actually lives off-board, as opposed to having to put it on the car, because it's not a real-time task related to the driving. That's one example of something that we do off-board. There are all these debates that go on on Twitter around self-driving. I can think of end-to-end versus the more kind of modular approach, or camera-only versus an array of sensors. I can't tell: are these debates actually interesting to an expert in the field, or do you think these are just settled matters and they're just grist for the algorithm? I understand where the questions are coming from. I do find that often the way they are posed and the way the debate happens, it is losing a lot of the nuance and detail that really matters. To me, the most interesting technical questions are at that level. The way we think about building the Waymo driver, it starts with a large off-board foundation model. You can imagine building a big model that understands how the physical world works and understands the important properties of what it means to drive—the social aspects of driving, and what it means to be a good driver as opposed to a bad one. So that's the foundation. Then we specialize it into three main off-board teachers. They are still large high-capacity off-board models: there's the Waymo driver, there is the simulator, and then there's the critic. Those then get distilled into smaller models that you can run inference on faster. The Waymo driver becomes the ML backbone of what's in the car. The simulator, of course, is what powers our synthetic generative environment that can run on the cloud for training and for evaluation in closed loop with the system. Sorry, does the simulator ever run locally? No, it doesn't. However, what I think is interesting is the way the decoder works—the way the model works. If you think about the generative task in the simulator of creating those realistic worlds and how other people behave—how cars, pedestrians, cyclists move—and the task that you have to solve on the car in real-time, there is this fundamental shared capability. It is understanding how these objects relate to each other and predicting what they might do in the future if you are running on the car, and then generating or sampling those probabilistic behaviors in the simulator. It's different models, but there is this shared foundation model that is able to power both. Similarly, if you think about the critic, the job of the critic is to find interesting events and then be opinionated about what's good behavior and what's bad behavior. It is the same fundamental understanding. If you're running inference on the car, you still have to figure out which of the multiple hypotheses of these future worlds you want to take action to steer towards. Okay, and these are all downstream of the same foundation model? That's right. So you start with a foundation model, then you specialize and fine-tune still off-board models—those are the teachers—and then you distill each one of the teachers. Each teacher kind of trains its own student: the driver, the simulator, the critic. You started working on self-driving 20 years ago. As you think about the tech evolution, is this just a scaling laws story where we had to be able to throw enough compute at it? Were there architectural approaches we needed to wait to have be invented? Was it just a story of we needed 20 years of going down the wrong cul-de-sacs before we eventually arrived at the right approach? Could you—knowing what you know now—could you have had a successful Waymo in market in 2015 or was there some enabling technology? No. Technology breakthroughs that happened over the years were critically important. Primarily in AI, but also in other areas like compute—the heavy compute that you need on board. I wouldn't characterize it as going through a thousand different dead ends and then having to retract and find the one right path. I would characterize it as iterative learning and evolution. Then Transformers came around, but Transformers, for example, are a very general architecture. It powers LLMs, it powers our models. But how you apply them to that space—I mean, this is where—it didn't just fall out of Transformers. Exactly. And of course people like to talk about architectures, and architecture is important, but really a lot of it comes down primarily to your metrics, your evaluation mechanisms, and all of the training recipes and data. LLMs are good at text or tokens specifically, and obviously perform best at domains that have some kind of single corpus of text they can work on—like coding, where it's very helpful that everything was textual already. Part of the success has been creating textual representations for domains such that we can then put LLMs against them. Can you describe how you encode the world that you're seeing? I mean, are you just building a 3D model, like a 3D bitmap essentially? This is where I think we get a bit into this question of what is the interface between the encoder and the decoder parts. I think that touches also on the thing you flagged earlier where people like to debate end-to-end or not end-to-end. So, when we say end-to-end, what do we mean? We mean that it is some large ML model. Typically you don't build them monolithically; you have different parts and different subgraphs. But what's important is that you can backprop the gradient and the loss function all through the different layers so that at every layer you can learn the weights and the representations that matter for the final task. You don't force it through some narrow funnel between, let's say, the encoder and the decoder. Yeah, I think of a simple view of end-to-end being, you know, pixels go in and car actions come out, which maybe is a bit of an oversimplification. That's exactly right. And if this is kind of the basic vanilla version of it, think about what it will take to build a driver that's capable of fully autonomous operations. If you think about this entire ecosystem of the driver, the simulator, the critic—if that's all you do, pixels in, trajectories out—it becomes very difficult to achieve the high level of safety and performance that we require. It becomes very difficult to kind of do it at scale. However, it's a very easy way to get started. You collect some data, kind of analogous to the LLM world. The easiest thing you can do is take a VLM. It already has a language-aligned camera encoder, and then it has a decoder that can predict or generate text. You can fine-tune it and say, "Hey, instead of text, generate trajectories." Very, very doable. In fact, we a while ago published a paper called EMMA that did exactly that. And it will actually, in the nominal case, drive pretty darn well, which is mind-blowingly impressive. That is very funny. You're saying you can take an off-the-shelf model which has nothing to do with driving to start with and you'll get these good results. That's right. You'll get—in the nominal case. I just want to be clear, it's orders of magnitude away from what you need. You should not try it on the street, but it works. It’s like the talking horse. It's impressive that it's talking. Exactly. And if the product that you wanted to build was maybe a driver assist system, not a fully autonomous system, then maybe that's all you need to do. For that you don't need all this other machinery of the simulator and the critic because the number of nines is drastically lower. But there is some intuition behind why that works. If you think about the hard parts of driving, it's not unlike having a conversation. Except in the LLM world, right, you're modeling language or maybe modeling a dialogue in the space of sentences and words. What makes driving hard is also this multi-agent social interactive part of it. If I do something, that's going to affect you, and that's going to affect somebody else. And the history matters; it's not local and just geometric. Context matters, semantics matter. It's in a different language—it's in the language of kind of body language if you will. We see that empirically validated if you do this approach. Okay, so then let's say we build this thing. Just camera, camera encoder, pixels go in, trajectory goes out. The quality is sufficient to drive in the nominal case. It's not sufficient to deal with the long tail of all the edge cases and hit the high bar of superhuman safety that we require. So then you start asking the question: what else do you need? If all you did was observe how other people drive when you trained the system—maybe observing passively how people drive and interact, maybe driving the car yourself and then using imitative learning to train it—you find that that's not enough. You have to do something in closed loop. You kind of have to do things like RLHF, which is also parallel to what we saw in the LLM world: Reinforcement Learning based fine-tuning, similar to reinforcement learning with human feedback. You want to do proper closed-loop driving where you explore all kinds of different situations and then you give it a reward signal to keep it in distribution. For that, then you need a realistic simulator. You also, if you want to have a good RL system, need to have an opinion for the reward function. This is where the critic comes in. If you have a purely end-to-end system, let's look at the simulator. What do you do? You're then constrained to just go from pixels to trajectories. That's all you can run the system on, and it's a very high-dimensional space so it's a hard problem to generate everything. Even if you solve that, it just becomes incredibly inefficient to run it in the full way of pixels to trajectories in simulation for training or for evaluation. This is when intermediate representations come in. There are some intermediate representations in this task in the physical world we know are correct. They're not sufficient, but they're not generality limiting. In other words, there is an object here, there's a concept of a road, there's signs, there's speed limits. So this is where augmenting that learned representation—those learned embeddings from the encoder-decoder—with that more structured representation is what we do. We find that this gives us additional knobs to simulate in that space, not just pixels to trajectories. It allows us to have additional safety validation layers in real-time and it gives us an additional mechanism to specify the reward function for evaluation in the critic or for training. So this is again, we've gone full circle. Is it end-to-end? Yes it is. But if you want to do it at scale for full autonomy, it's augmented with all of this other stuff. That's very interesting on the simulating point. It's just very hard to simulate for an end-to-end model because it's easier to deal in intermediate representation rather than coming up with the pixel-perfect view of the world. You need both. Having end-to-end architecture that's augmented with that structure allows you to kind of play in both of those worlds. What are you looking to do as a self-driving car? I mean it sounds funny, but I think people maybe don't realize that there are many different things that you're looking to solve for. You're looking to get the person to their destination reasonably promptly, drive smoothly, have many nines of safety, and also not annoy other drivers and get honked at. So what are some of the reward functions or things you're optimizing for that maybe are not obvious to people? Safety is the primary focus, right? But of course we also want to be a smooth driver—for both people in the car and other actors—and we also want to be a predictable, well-behaved one so that it can nicely fit into the whole social ecosystem of our roadways. It seems like one of the issues that has quickly emerged with self-driving is the fact that people can't have nice things, or not everyone is nice to the robots. Whether you're driving through a dodgy area or getting blocked or maybe I'm going to go around the block and drop you somewhere better, these are all, as you say, human issues. How do you go about solving those? A lot of the ones that you mentioned are just things that we need to work on. Understanding that if we're not dropping you off exactly where you wanted to be dropped off or we don't give you a good interface to tell us, that's on us. We've got to make it better. I feel like the drop-off is actually a pretty nuanced part of the self-driving journey. Like the highway stuff and the 35-mile-an-hour roads—that is all nailed—but there's just like a lot of nuance in the drop-off experience. I'd say they're all hard. You picked freeways and you picked drop-offs for different reasons. For drop-offs, it's understanding where you want to go, making it as convenient as possible for you. And pickups and drop-offs—it's not exactly symmetrical. But then there's also understanding the context of the situation. Where do you stop? You don't want to block a driveway, you don't want to double park, although in some cases where if it's a quick one maybe it's okay. So there's a lot of nuance that goes into doing that well so that it's a frictionless experience for the rider as well as other folks. Freeways—most of the time nothing much happens. They're very well structured because we design them that way. But there is still that long tail of really complicated stuff that happens where the consequences of a bad event are much more severe. Speed is much higher; everything's quadratic in speed. But we see a lot of stuff. Imagine grills falling off of freeways, imagine people getting into accidents and kind of spinning out of control. You see one of those flatbed trucks with just a bunch of stuff piled in it and you're driving behind it. I don't know, I always find it very nerve-wracking. We've seen them leave a trail. Yeah, exactly. But it feels like the general sentiment with Waymo is that the driving has mostly now been solved by you guys and it's kind of a question of scaling up and maybe some super long-tail stuff, really snowy conditions. Is that your sense internally or is there actually much more nuance to it than that? I would say that it's not like we're done with engineering. I would say that we've clearly moved past the stage of scientific research and deep core technology development to this new phase of accelerated global scaling and deployment. So you know we still have work to do, but I don't see today any limitations or any gaps in the core technology. The core technology is good enough now. I can't think of any aspect of driving that is not supported by the fundamental technology. Now that said, there is a lot of work to do in specialization and in validation before we can deploy responsibly. We're not driving everywhere in the world. We are planning to start operating in London and in Tokyo this year. Do you have a driver that you're using today in San Francisco that we can just plop down in London and go? No. But what we're seeing is incredibly encouraging from the perspective of whether the core technology is there. Now it's a matter of collecting the data, doing some specialization and validation. I mean signs are different, and in both of those places people drive on the other side of the road. But that's actually not that hard for computers. Core technology generalizes really well, but you still have work that you have to do. What generalizes least well? We're increasingly finding, especially now that we're able to hook the Waymo AI to the AI in the digital world and the VLMs and kind of inherit the general world knowledge from VLMs, we're seeing really strong results from zero-shot or few-shot learning. But there are a few things like cold winter weather, where it affects the entire stack. So it's not just the AI, but you actually need the hardware—you need to have the proper cleaning solutions, heating elements—and then think about things that are completely solvable by computers, like motion control on slippery surfaces. That takes a bunch of work. You don't get that for free from just pulling in some VLM decoder. Was it the case—my impression not knowing anything—is that in the early days there was maybe a lot of San Francisco specific work or Phoenix specific work in the early markets? Whether it be mapping or something else, you guys seem to either have solved that in generalizing it or just scaled up your ability to do the city-specific work. What enabled the rapid city expansion? We usually think about it as the capability of the Waymo driver as well as deployment. We don't think primarily and directly in that space of cities or zip codes. We think about the operating domain: freeways, cold weather, snow, rain, fog, density, etc. And then that maps to the city. A particular city would be within the operating domain or outside of it. If we rewind history a little bit, our initial deployment where we started offering a fully autonomous commercial service for the first time was in 2020 in Chandler, Arizona. And that was on what we call the fourth generation of the Waymo driver. This was the Pacifica minivan with different hardware and software. There we were super focused on kind of doing the whole thing end-to-end: learn how to build the driver, evaluate it, deploy regularly, operate end-to-end 24/7 with customers, and learn from the customers. We were very focused on that operating domain of mostly Chandler, which is a medium-low complexity one. Then when we made the jump to the fifth generation of our system—this is what's on the I-Pace today—we really wanted to take a huge bite out of that operating domain. And we collected data all over the United States. All different states, different cities. When we chose to deploy in the hardest parts of San Francisco and hardest parts of Phoenix, we made a big jump on the hardware side and most importantly on the software, the AI side. I would say that was the big discontinuous jump. And that's what you're seeing now after we've scaled up and iterated on all of the aspects of building and deploying the driver. So Driver Version 5 was just a much more generalizable stack than Version 4? And what was it about it? Was it just that it had been trained on a much wider dataset? It was when we made this big bet on AI. There were a lot more little AI models and ML models in the fourth generation. We made a much bigger bet and jump to AI as the backbone for the fifth generation. And that's been—so we made that jump and then we've been iterating and improving the model since then. Could we go down hardware a second? So lots of hardware questions, but one is maybe—everyone in this space has a very charismatic demo of a vehicle that is custom-made for self-driving. You know, it's often the van with the no steering wheel, seats facing in both directions. You guys have one, Tesla has the steering wheel-less Cybercab, Cruise had the Cruise Origin. And yet we're still driving in Jaguars that have a steering wheel in the front and are pretty similar to consumer cars. It's interesting to me because if we were talking about this 10 years ago we might say, "Well yeah developing a custom car, that's relatively straightforward—we know how to put a bunch of sensors on a new car—but the software will take a long time." What's interesting is we've made huge progress in the software, but the cars are still derivatives of cars that people are driving. I'm curious why you think the custom hardware has not happened as of 2026? It's obviously a small improvement compared to the big improvement, but it's just interesting that it still has to happen. Well I would say our sixth generation of the vehicle and the driver is our version of that. And this is all-I platform, right? That is—it still has the—we can talk about whether you want to have the seats pointed backwards or not. I actually think it looks nice in a demo, but practically speaking maybe not the way to go. But that is—it is a custom-designed vehicle and we put a lot of thought into moving away from a car that's designed around the driver to a car that's designed around the passenger. It's much more spacious. And it's happening. We're not open to the public yet, but I took a ride in it the other day fully autonomously and that's coming this year. How much better is it as a passenger experience? You'll tell me once you give it a try. I love it. It's all about the space and the convenience of ingress and egress and the screens and the interface to the passenger. So we put a lot of thought into every aspect of it. It has sliding doors, it's very easy to get in, it has a flat floor. If you sit in the back you can fully stretch out and there's so much space there. And it looks from the outside, it looks fairly big, right? But the actual footprint of that is barely, barely, barely larger than the I-Pace. So it's kind of amazing that you walk in and it feels like you're in a living room. I guess my question is just, Waymo does 25 million rides a year run rate-ish with the Jaguar I-Pace. It's interesting that so much scaling has happened with self-driving so far on the old retrofit—maybe that's to be expected. I don't think it was a given. You're right. But if you think about the value proposition, of course there is the safety of it. There's also the privacy—being in the car by yourself, maybe with other folks, but not having to share that space with another human. Waymo’s a great product. But I guess this is why we're seeing such consistency. The car drives well, very predictable. And you can go beyond that. You can specialize even more to make the experience even more magical around the rider. But I guess it would have been disappointing if without the specialized car, and I think I would have been surprised if we leveled off at some other much lower level of customer adoption. A car seems more of an optimization improvement, but the core of the value proposition comes from those other factors. I guess it's just take risk on one thing at a time. We'll start by doing the software layer and then we'll build the specialized car or something like that. Exactly. It's a big investment. So you have to de-risk the fundamentals. Throughout our history we were very focused on setting the biggest goal for the company to de-risk the most important questions. We talked about the third generation where we wanted to deploy something and go end-to-end; we talked about what was the goal with the fifth generation, and then there's the sixth generation. So what is the sixth generation? Where it made sense to go and spend all this effort into the custom vehicle? And the sixth generation is both the custom vehicle, is it also a new generation of the driving stack? It is the new hardware—the sensors—the self-driving hardware that we're putting on the OHAI vehicle is the sixth generation. It is very different from the fifth generation. It is simpler, it is more capable, it is much lower cost. It’s like a fraction of the cost. It’s comparable to what you would get in a fancy ADAS system nowadays, a driver assist system. The software is pretty much the same. So that's another—when we talk about generalizability of the Waymo driver, we talk about weather conditions, we talk about cities, but it also generalizes well to different vehicle platforms and different sensor configurations. What is different about the sixth generation hardware stack and how did you make it cheaper? It still has the same three sensing modalities but we've made significant optimizations in all three. Unification, simplification, and there is just, you know, just riding the commodity curve. Is it a classic case of manufacturing scale where we're now being able to buy more of these? Well, scale hasn't fully come into play, but all of those—if you think about the supply chains, the industries—cameras are pretty mature. Radars, way many years ago, used to be bulky, complex, very expensive when we were putting them in planes, but then we started putting them on cars. Now you can get a decent automotive radar for tens of dollars. There is a variant of the automotive radar called the imaging radar; it gives you a richer—so that also has come down in cost drastically but it's a little bit behind your standard automotive radars. Lidars are following the same very predictable trend. So we're riding that and we're also learning from the previous generation to just make improvements and simplifications and optimizations. Very silly question: what are lidars versus radars better at in a self-driving context? They're very complementary. Lidar, it's all blasting photons out there and then they bounce off of something and come back. You measure what comes back. The frequencies are very different. So laser gives you—it's very, very high resolution. You can think of it as like a laser beam that goes out, spins around, it shoots out millions of these laser pulses per second, and then each one comes back and you are sampling the 3D structure of the world with very high resolution. So lidar for very fine-grain map. That’s right. Radar has much lower resolution but because of the physics of it, it can—it degrades much better in adverse weather conditions. So fog, snow, heavy rain, it wouldn't be occluded by particles between it and the target. Exactly. So imagine driving on a freeway—radar will give you really good returns for cars that are absolutely invisible in the camera space. Oh that's interesting. So does that mean there are some environments where you'll be relying significantly more on radar? It's a combination of the sensors, right? We rely on—each one is noisy. How the noise characteristics show up in different environments is different. But it's not like we switch from one to another. It's not like we estimate what's happening with the world through cameras and then through radar and then through lidar and then we compare. No, they're like—there's an encoder for camera, there's an encoder for lidar, and they all go into the system that gives you jointly the best view of what's happening in the world around you. If it's a nice bright sunny day, cameras are very valuable. If it's pitch dark, or you have sun in your face or you're blinded by the headlights from an oncoming car, then camera will degrade. Radar and lidar are completely unaffected. Are there technical problems that are your white whale or you're just still chasing or you are particularly interested in solving? Even if they're kind of niche for the—you know—we just really want to have driving when it's actually snowing nailed, or steep hills in San Francisco? I am super excited right now about the accelerating global expansion. More cities in the United States and going international. Being—I understand my answer, your question about the technology, I'll come back to that, but really that's the thing that I am today most excited about. Just getting to a place where in any major metropolitan area you can fly into the airport and then take a Waymo and go anywhere you want to go—that is insanely exciting to me right now. So technically what I'm most excited about is all of the rapid progress in AI and the world models, the foundation model work. It is just such a massive boost to how much we can simplify the system, how much we can bring down the cost and how we can scale globally. And there's just some magic that happens that I don't think I would have anticipated a few years ago. That I find from the technical perspective just insanely thrilling. When you talk about the progress in AI, what are the most fun parts of it for you these days? I think it's seeing the capability and the scaling laws from this approach of starting with that cornerstone of the foundation model and then specializing to teachers and then distilling. You get such big wins in performance across the board. You invest something into the architecture or get better data or training recipes at that early stage and it just has massive amplification and ripple effects out. In some ways it is kind of magical. And then you see it on the car. I've had some moments where the car does something, and you look at a log, and I've been surprised. Like it does things that I didn't think it was capable of doing. When you see emergent behavior, that's kind of a proud moment? One example, yeah. You build a system and you think you understand how it works and you understand fully the limits of its capability, and then it does something kind of almost magical. It's exhilarating. One example I can give you was this example where the situation happened in San Francisco—a fairly benign situation where at an intersection our light is red. There's cross traffic, a bus goes by, and it stops partially blocking the way. Our light turns green, so we start to go, we're nudging around the bus, and then you see a pedestrian being detected on the other side of the bus. And then the car responds appropriately. It slows down, goes a little bit wider, and then a pedestrian actually emerges from the bus and we go on our own way. So the first time I looked at that log, I was like "What's going on here?" I know we have pretty darn good sensors and the software is very capable, but like, we don't see through stuff. It saw the pedestrian through the bus? Through—it saw the pedestrian on the other side of the bus. And it's not like you look at the windows—radar shouldn't—it's a massive metal box. You look at the sensor data and it shouldn't—radar shouldn't be able to go through it. In the camera, you can't see because there's reflections and there's people on the bus. So what is going on? Maybe it's noise or some coincidence. And I, you know, first time I saw it I couldn't actually believe it. I was like "No, no, no, something doesn't sound right." What actually turned out was happening is that our peripheral lidars bounced under the bus and there was just a little bit of very, very noisy reflection of the movement of the person's feet that was enough for the AI models to say "Hey, likely there's a pedestrian there." And moreover, there's enough data there to predict what they're going to do. And it just kind of blew my mind. Is this the perfect example to explain what we were talking about earlier—the value of one fusion across a sensor suite, but then secondly building an intermediate representation of what's going on? If you're just dealing with pixels—I mean the person behind the bus does not exist in pixel space and so you need to have some representation of the world that exists to be able to reason about the person behind the bus. I think it's an example where using that intermediate representation to boost the level of performance of all parts of the model is what's happening here. Imagine solving this problem with a black box purely open-loop imitative system. It would be—it's not impossible, but in practice what would it take to achieve that level of performance? Very, very difficult. What metrics can you share on just where the business is at today in terms of rides, revenues, cars on the road? We have about 3,000 cars on the roads. We're doing about half a million rides per week. That translates to over 4 million fully autonomous miles per week. We are operating in a fully autonomous mode in 11 cities in the US; 10 of those we have public riders. What's the ghost city? The ghost city is Nashville. We just started there. We just opened it up to riders in four new cities in one day. That was one of those little but super exciting moments where I thought back to the history. How long did it take us from the first time we started fully autonomous rider-only operation to the first time we had external riders in four cities? That was about eight years. And then just the other week we just launched four in one day. It seems now clear that in 15 years most miles that are driven will be autonomous. There'll be some burn-in period. Some of that will be by Level 4, Level 5 systems expanding in new cities. Some of it will be existing driver assist systems getting more and more capable. What do you think that working your way up from the lower levels versus working your way expanding from existing products like Waymo will look like? Because we're going to eat it from both sides. I don't believe we will. I actually think this—cars will get smarter. There is going to be advances in driver assist systems. And there is going to be at the same time from Level 4 autonomy, simplification, and the sensors of today are not going to be the sensors of tomorrow. They'll be much more integrated, simpler, and much lower cost. So from that perspective there is a path of convergence. And there's also a path of convergence from the product lines. Ride-hailing today—eventually it'll be on your personal car. If you talk about the technology, I see it as fundamentally two different problems. There are driver assist systems and then there is full autonomy. I think it's deceptive to think of them as incremental on one spectrum of complexity. Okay, but you think one cannot work one's way up from driver assist systems to full self-driving? You think you have to start building a full self-driving system? I think you have to tackle—if I think about the hardest parts of building a fully autonomous rider-only system, they are very different from what you do for a driver assist system. I know some work in this space helps you, but it is a qualitative jump. When can I buy a Waymo so that I don't need to wait for it when I want to go? I can just, when I'm ready, walk out the door and it's there? I'm not going to give you a date today, but you are not the first person to bring this up as a product request. I'll add it to the list. Just, you know, waiting for the car—it'd be nice to just have it in the garage there and keep your stuff in it. It's not the first time you've heard that request? It seems to me operationally very intensive and very hard. A self-driving car is actually not self-driving; it takes a village. You have all of the human operators ready to step in, and there was that thundering herd incident that highlighted that for people. And then there's just keeping the cars clean and keeping everything running. Can you describe what the operational infrastructure that sits behind Waymo looks like? I will say that we are overall in all of those areas on a path of increasing efficiency and automation. The number of manual steps that one had to do five years ago to launch a Waymo versus where we are today is drastically different. Nowadays, our depots are like a fully automatically orchestrated dance of autonomous vehicles. The way it looks today is cars will automatically go on their own to pick up their riders. If for some reason they need to come back—maybe they're low on energy, maybe somebody left a mess in the car—they will automatically come to the depot. Cleaning today is a manual process, so it'll get flagged in the car. We have fleet management systems say "Hey car number 378 needs cleaning," and actually on the sensor dome we're able to display icons so it'll show you like a little emoji. And you know, people whose job it is to clean the car still come and clean it up. If cleaning is not required and it's just charging, we'll automatically pull into a charging stall and the car will say "Hey I need charging." We don't yet have automated charging. You can in the future imagine that being fully automated, right? But a person will come in and plug in a cable, the car will charge, and then say "Hey, now I'm ready to go" and it'll get unplugged and the car will pull out of the parking stall and then go on its merry way. One of the new Porsches has inductive charging where you just drive over the charging mat. I was amazed that that works at car scale, but presumably in the future they'll just be able to drive onto the mat. Or do you think robotic plug-in will be easier? We'll see. I think there's some questions about efficiency and how that plays into the overall cost and which one will be most cost-beneficial. How well-behaved is the Waymo riding population in terms of not leaving a mess in the car? We have wonderful riders. The most amazing customers in the world. Generally I'd say they are very good. There is something about not having a person in the car—it's not somebody else's car. In some ways you kind of want to preserve the—I think generally people want to kind of preserve the nice aspects of it. It's a broken windows thing—it's so clean to begin with that—I know, yeah. I think that's the general trend that we see. It feels like it's your own, right? So you don't want to mess up your own space. However I will say that it varies and you can imagine, you know, a college town on a Saturday night and that's a different distribution. Will I be able to get a Waymo at any address that has USPS service in the US? Eventually it will, absolutely. There's no doubt in my mind. I think it's just a matter of when and what modality would make the most commercial sense—ride share versus privately owned. For ride, it's not a technical problem—technology is solved—but if there's just not enough density of trips, does it make sense? Probably not, right? They can be deployed somewhere else and you probably don't want a horribly bad ETA. This is where a personally owned vehicle that is equipped with the Waymo driver is maybe how you will see it materialized. What will the second-order effects of say majority autonomous traffic be? It feels like a lot of things will work better where when someone merges into a lane very poorly and everyone all the way back has to slam on the brakes, that's kind of anti-social behavior. So it feels like higher quality and pro-social driving will just reduce traffic a little bit even for the same number of cars on the road. But presumably there'll be other second-order effects—like we'll want higher throughput traffic lights? The first thing I think you mentioned is a huge deal. I mean just think about traffic jams. What's that saying that the Navy SEALs use? "Slow is smooth and smooth is fast." Traffic jams are often like you accelerate abruptly then you come to a stop. Sometimes you have a traffic jam—like what happened? Well, an old lady crossed the road three hours ago and we still have the standing wave. If everybody was a smooth, predictable, and consistent driver, then you wouldn't have those traffic jams. But longer term, things like parking lots. Right now, if you look at what our most interesting pieces of land are allocated to—it's parking lots, it's garages. And why is that? Well, because again your car is just sitting there 90% of the time. If more cars become fully autonomous then there's no need, right? And then imagine just imagine what you can do with your favorite city in the world if you don't have to spend that huge fraction of it on just keeping these chunks of metal sitting around. Yeah, I don't think people often realize how big a deal parking minimums are for the layout of the urban landscape. The coffee shop near where I am would like to have outdoor seating but can't because it would reclaim parking spots. Wouldn't it be wonderful? I only have two more questions. But I'm curious to talk about Google's relationship with self-driving where, again, it feels like right now Waymo is aside from everything else AI related kind of the most exciting thing happening at Google. But it was a very long journey to get here. I feel like you could say that Google almost started working on it too early because you were saying there's been a bunch of recent enabling technologies. And so, did it require Google starting when it did so early, or could one have spun up this project in 2015 or 2020? How did Google keep the faith when it always felt like it was perennially two years away? Yeah, on the latter part, I just have to give credit—huge kudos and gratitude to Larry and Sergey and Alphabet leadership in general. It is part of the culture and the DNA of the company to have that vision and have the stamina and conviction to go the distance. To the other part of the question, was it too early? I don't know. I think what we've been seeing clearly is all of the breakthroughs that we've seen over the years have changed how we're building the system, but the complexity of the problem is such that you need to go through these iterative cycles, right? It's not yet still—and we've seen many waves of technology. Breakthroughs in 2013, ImageNet came around, and there was this air of, "Okay, that is the right time to start a self-driving company." Then Transformers came around and VLMs, and all of those are super powerful and, you know, have applications in other spaces like the AI in the digital world. They certainly have an impact on our AI in the physical world. But they're no silver bullets, right? They kind of drastically reshape that early part of the curve. It's always been the nature of this problem. It's very easy to get started. It's deceptively easy to get started. But it is super hard to go the full distance and get that case—the number of nines, right? You have the standard engineering rule of thumb that every next nine takes, you know, 10x more effort. So maybe there is a more optimal path, but I don't see there is some magical moment where the true complexity of the problem goes away and then you can just take some off-the-shelf components and your business. If that were the case, then I think the industry would look very different today. The last question I have: you've been promoted a lot at Google. It feels like Google really recognized your talents. Just what do you think Google does well? But just what have you observed firsthand from how Google does this so well? I would say Google—that culture of Google of not accepting the status quo, having a big vision, and investing in technical talent and the people who can, you know, go the distance and realize the vision, that is part of the culture. I think this is what you're seeing with the breakthroughs in AI in the digital world and like all of the early investments in Transformers and on the other fundamental technologies. Quantum computing and, you know, I guess we're not unlike those efforts as well. Dmitri, thank you. Yeah, thank you. As we're seeing with Waymo rolling out widespread autonomy, it has second-order changes on the entire system—in this case traffic patterns or other drivers' behavior, or eventually how cities are laid out. And autonomous systems are coming in many domains. In commerce, soon agents are going to be transacting without human intervention. We're basically getting driverless commerce. And Stripe is building the economic infrastructure for AI. And as part of that, we're letting payments be initiated by humans or by agents. So if you want to sell to agents or if you want to let your agent spend money all around the web, check out Stripe's agentic commerce suite.

The Future of AI & AGI: General Magic founder Marc Porat on Sovereign AI and the Next Revolution

SandboxAQ(224.0K) ·33:09 ·73.7K views · 2026-03-24
Marc Porat (Founder, General Magic)
Artificial General Intelligence (AGI) represents a "new species" of unbounded, recursive intelligence that will drive a societal shift toward total abundance, provided the world adopts Sovereign AI to preserve cultural identity and deploys modular nu...
TL;DR Artificial General Intelligence (AGI) represents a "new species" of unbounded, recursive intelligence that will drive a societal shift toward total abundance, provided the world adopts Sovereign AI to preserve cultural identity and deploys modular nuclear energy to sustain...

TL;DR

Artificial General Intelligence (AGI) represents a "new species" of unbounded, recursive intelligence that will drive a societal shift toward total abundance, provided the world adopts Sovereign AI to preserve cultural identity and deploys modular nuclear energy to sustain massive data center demands.

The Evolution of the Personal Digital Interface

  • The General Magic Legacy: Marc Porat recounts his 1988 vision at Apple, where he proposed a Venn diagram of "Computer, Communication, and Content" that predicted the smartphone 20 years before its ubiquity.
      • The "General Magic" Alumni: The team Porat assembled included the architects of the modern tech landscape: Tony Fadell (iPhone), Andy Rubin (Android), Megan Smith (former US CTO), and Kevin Lynch (Apple Watch).
  • The Shift to "Me" Computing: In the original smartphone vision, "You" was at the center. Porat predicts that in the next decade, AI will evolve into a "digital twin" or hyper-personalized "me" that follows users across all devices and environments.
  • Ubiquity Over Devices: Hardware will become a commodity; the profound revolution lies in the compute and memory that establishes a permanent, additive relationship with the user.

Sovereign AI and Cultural Authenticity

  • The English-Centric Bias: Current frontier Large Language Models (LLMs) are approximately 90% trained on English and American cultural data.
      • The Loss of Nuance: Porat argues that while translations are brilliant, they lack the "authenticity, nuance, and contradictions" of native cultures, effectively erasing thousands of years of civilization from the AI's core logic.
  • Defining Sovereign AI: Beyond data governance and national security, true Sovereign AI must embrace a nation’s specific "corpora"—everything from 900 BCE Mayan codices to modern street slang.
  • Sector-Specific Impact:
      • Healthcare: Porat highlights a vision where a personalized, culturally-rooted AI acts as "100 doctors" for every citizen, synthesizing millions of medical journals to provide hyper-local health advice (e.g., detecting a dengue outbreak via local blogs).
      • Education: Similar to how Steve Jobs put Macs in schools, providing youth with AI in their own language and culture will create a workforce with "powerful, portable skills."

The Arrival of AGI: A "New Species"

  • The AGI Timeline: Porat suggests that for many frontier models and users, AGI (Artificial General Intelligence) will arrive within the next 1–2 years.
      • The Turing Test: He notes that the Turing Test was effectively passed in 2025, though society barely noticed the milestone.
  • Unbounded Intelligence: AGI is characterized by being self-recursive—it teaches itself, decides its own goals, and seeks out the information it needs to grow exponentially.
  • The "Utopian/Dystopian" Duality:
      • Utopia: Access to a "team of geniuses" in every field for $20/month, leading to the eradication of disease and the achievement of "healthspan" (longevity with health).
      • Dystopia: History shows that when a more intelligent civilization or species encounters a less intelligent one, the latter is often "crushed." Porat warns that we are creating a "synthetic new species" that humans have never had to compete with before.

The Energy Crisis and the Nuclear Solution

  • The Data Center Threat: AI data centers are currently the "biggest enemy of climate change" due to unsustainable energy consumption.
      • The Sustainability Gap: Projections suggest 12% of US energy will soon go toward AI, a figure that is not sustainable under current power grids.
  • The Nuclear Mandate: Porat argues that efficiency gains in chips (like NVIDIA’s) and software are not enough to offset the demand slope.
      • The Micro-Modular Revolution: He advocates for Micro-Modular Reactors (MMRs) or Small Modular Reactors (SMRs)—1-gigawatt units built in factories and dropped onto campuses to provide local, resilient power.
      • Global Competition: China is currently building one large nuclear power plant per month, while the US has built zero in the last decade, creating a massive strategic energy gap.

NVIDIA GTC Telecom Special Address: The AI Grid—Intelligently Connecting AI Infrastructure

NVIDIA(2.2M) ·40:57 ·5.3K views · 2026-03-24
Ronnie Vasishta (SVP, Telecom, NVIDIA)
The telecommunications industry is entering a "generational opportunity" to build the **AI Grid**, a distributed infrastructure that merges the telecom and AI stacks into a unified, orchestrated platform.
TL;DR The telecommunications industry is entering a "generational opportunity" to build the AI Grid, a distributed infrastructure that merges the telecom and AI stacks into a unified, orchestrated platform. By leveraging existing physical assets and transition to AI-native 6G,...

TL;DR

The telecommunications industry is entering a "generational opportunity" to build the AI Grid, a distributed infrastructure that merges the telecom and AI stacks into a unified, orchestrated platform. By leveraging existing physical assets and transition to AI-native 6G, telcos can shift from selling "bits" to "tokens," powering the next era of physical and edge AI.

The Three Tectonic Forces Shaping Telecom

NVIDIA SVP Ronnie Vasishta identifies a convergence of three massive industry shifts that necessitate a new infrastructure model:

  • Accelerated Compute: A transition from 60 years of general-purpose compute to accelerated architectures, delivering a million-fold performance increase over the last decade.
  • The Evolution of AI: AI is moving beyond perception and generative models into "Physical AI," where agents interact with the real world in real-time.
  • AI-Native 6G: Unlike previous generations, 6G is being designed as the "fabric of AI," built specifically to handle the high-concurrency, low-latency demands of distributed intelligence.

From "Bits" to "Tokenomics": The New Revenue Model

Vasishta argues that telcos must rethink their monetization strategies by focusing on the value of the AI output rather than raw data throughput.

  • Token-Aware Infrastructure: Future networks will be measured by "tokenomics"—metrics like time-to-first-token, uplink tokens, and tokens per second—which dictate the quality of AI experiences.
  • Edge Proximity Advantage: By running applications on the AI Grid rather than the centralized cloud, telcos can drastically reduce latency and jitter.
      • Example: Personal AI assistants running on the grid showed near-instant response times compared to multi-second pauses when cloud-based.
  • Vision AI & Concurrency: Vision Language Models (VLMs) are identified as the fastest-growing application area, allowing cameras to trigger autonomous actions without human monitoring.

Strategic Partnerships and Early Adopters

Several major providers are already deploying NVIDIA-based AI Grid architectures to solve specific industry challenges:

  • AT&T & Cisco (IoIT): Teaming up to transform the Internet of Things into the "Internet of Intelligent Things" using RTX 6000 Ada GPUs for public safety applications in Dallas.
  • T-Mobile (AI-RAN): In San Jose, T-Mobile is using AI-RAN (Radio Access Network) infrastructure to run vision analytics for traffic intersections, achieving a 5x improvement in reaction speed for anomaly detection.
  • Comcast & Spectrum: Utilizing the AI Grid for hyper-personalized advertising and providing low-latency 3D rendering services (under 10ms) for movie studios, respectively.
  • Indosat Ooredoo Hutchison (IOH): Deploying the AI Grid across Indonesia to provide localized AI services in agriculture, healthcare, and education to close the digital divide.

AI-RAN and the AI-Native 6G Roadmap

The convergence of the compute stack allows the Radio Access Network (RAN) to run alongside AI applications on the same hardware.

  • Software-Defined Infrastructure: By moving away from purpose-built custom silicon to accelerated compute, telcos can utilize a "single pane of glass" to orchestrate RAN and AI workloads based on real-time demand.
  • Aerial Omniverse Digital Twin: 6G will be "born in simulation." NVIDIA’s digital twin platform allows developers to simulate site-specific ray-tracing and antenna physics in a physics-accurate environment before deployment.
  • The 6G Commitment: NVIDIA and its partners have committed to building 6G on three core principles: Open (software-defined), Secure (end-to-end transparency), and Trusted.

Notable Quotes

"AI is redefining computing and driving the largest infrastructure buildout in human history—and telecommunications is next." — Ronnie Vasishta (quoting Jensen Huang)
"6G is being born in the era of AI. This is the first time we are able to build an AI-native 6G and deploy 6G to be the fabric of AI." — Ronnie Vasishta
"The AI-native telco journey is a journey... but it’s a generational opportunity. Do not let that opportunity pass. There has never been a better time to be in telcos." — Ronnie Vasishta
Hello everybody and welcome, as Kanika said, to GTC—the second day of GTC. We're going to be kicking off an amazing schedule of telecom announcements. What I thought I would do today is take a moment to really look in context at some of the very exciting areas of infrastructure build-outs. Yesterday, we saw perhaps the most exciting but also technologically advanced infrastructure that's leading the industry. The telecom industry cannot get left behind this. We are, what, three years into the AI revolution? And three years into this AI revolution, we are now seeing some of the biggest build-outs of infrastructure—IT infrastructure. But I'm here to tell you that there is now another additional infrastructure build-out that needs to happen. This is the infrastructure that connects the real physical world and deploys AI infrastructure into the hands of everyone on a large scale. That infrastructure is the responsibility of the telecoms, the cable MSOs, and the CDN providers to put in place to take this to the next level. We call this infrastructure the AI Grid. So it does seem, I must admit, that AI is everything everywhere all at once. I think we would be honest if we said some of it is a little bit overwhelming. But also, we know there's tremendous opportunity. How can an industry that traditionally hasn't been known for moving fast—there's murmurings, maybe some of you agree—not get overwhelmed by this rapid pace of innovation and opportunity in a highly regulated industry? We're seeing, though, new companies being built overnight with multi-billion dollar valuations. We're seeing older companies that have existed for a long time experiencing never-seen-before growth. The telecom industry cannot let this opportunity pass. This represents the greatest generational opportunity for telecoms, and we cannot let this moment pass. Think about this in context. There are three tectonic forces at play. We've heard a lot already about the transition from general-purpose compute to accelerated compute. This compute architecture that existed for 60 years is rapidly being transformed, seeing a million-x performance increase over the last 10 years. The alternative compute stack that's formed the basis of the cloud industry, which is the general-purpose compute stack, hasn't really had the capability to support the telecommunications compute stack. We have the ability to have agentic flows within the AI operations because now the RAN is all software-defined.
