Arm CEO on Selling Own Chips, Meta Is One of First Customers

Bloomberg Technology(719.0K) ·10:16 ·220 views · 2026-03-24
Rene Haas (CEO, Arm) Tom Mackenzie (Reporter, Bloomberg Technology)
Arm is pivoting from a pure IP licensing model to selling its own physical hardware with the launch of the Arm AGI CPU, securing Meta as its lead customer to capitalize on a projected $1 trillion total addressable market (TAM) by 2030.
TL;DR Arm is pivoting from a pure IP licensing model to selling its own physical hardware with the launch of the Arm AGI CPU, securing Meta as its lead customer to capitalize on a projected $1 trillion total addressable market (TAM) by 2030. Arm AGI CPU Launch: For the first...

TL;DR

Arm is pivoting from a pure IP licensing model to selling its own physical hardware with the launch of the Arm AGI CPU, securing Meta as its lead customer to capitalize on a projected $1 trillion total addressable market (TAM) by 2030.

Strategic Shift: From Blueprints to Physical Chips

  • Arm AGI CPU Launch: For the first time in its history, Arm is designing and manufacturing its own physical chips rather than just selling "blueprints" (IP).
      • Lead Customer: Meta is the primary partner and was a driving force behind the product's development.
      • Timeline: The chip is available now, with shipments expected to begin by the end of this year. Revenue will begin appearing on balance sheets within the current fiscal year.
  • Evolution of Business Model: The move follows a multi-year progression from selling raw IP to Compute Subsystems (CSS), and finally to integrated physical products.
  • Margin Strategy: While selling physical chips may lower gross margin *percentages* compared to pure IP licensing, Haas expects a massive increase in gross margin *dollars*.
      • Example: Haas noted that a 50% margin on a $100 billion market ($50B) is far more valuable than a 95% margin on a $3 billion market ($2.8B).

The AI Growth Engine: "Agentic AI" and Compute Demand

  • CPU Resurgence: While GPUs handle token generation, Haas emphasizes that CPUs are "first-class customers" in AI because they handle orchestration, scheduling, and distribution.
  • Workload Multiplier: Agentic AI—AI that can act autonomously—is currently driving a 4x increase in CPU workloads.
  • Market Projections: Arm forecasts the general-purpose CPU TAM will grow from roughly $60–$70 billion today to $100 billion by 2030.
  • Total Opportunity: Haas projects Arm’s total available market across all sectors (including automotive and edge devices) to reach $1 trillion by the end of the decade.

Supply Chain Constraints and Macro Outlook

  • Memory Shortages: High-bandwidth memory (HBM) and NAND are facing massive supply shortages. Haas expects the memory market to remain "quite supply-constrained" for the next 12 to 18 months.
  • Smartphone Resilience: Despite memory tightness, Haas is "bearish" on volume risks. Even a 15–20% drop in smartphone volumes would only result in a 1–2% impact on Arm’s royalties due to their shift toward higher-value Compute Subsystems (CSS).
  • Geopolitical Stability: Arm has not yet seen material financial impacts or supply chain disruptions resulting from conflicts in the Middle East.

Future Frontiers: Inference and Space

  • Shift to Inference: Haas views the industry shift from "training" models to "inference" (running models) as an "intellectually obvious" evolution that will lift demand across the entire ecosystem.
  • Data Centers in Space: Haas takes the concept of space-based data centers seriously, citing "profound" power benefits despite the obvious challenges of latency and hardware maintenance.
  • Security Infrastructure: As data centers house increasingly valuable IP, Haas confirms that physical and digital security for these facilities is becoming a top-tier industry priority.
"The compute demand for AI is off the charts. And Arm is at the heart of literally every AI compute workload." — Rene Haas
"One of the things to think about when you get into the chip business is the margin—gross margin percentage may be less, but the gross margin dollars may be much, much larger." — Rene Haas

When it comes to memory, high bandwidth memory, NAND and other parts of the memory space, massive supply shortages. Do you see a contraction of the smartphone market this year? It- it remains to be- until when do you see that- that tightness in particularly HBM memory, high bandwidth memory? Tightness, how- when do you see that being resolved? How many more years of this? Yeah, I think the memory market is going to be a- a tough market if you listen to SK Hynix or Samsung or Micron, the next year to 18 months, I think it's going to be quite supply constrained. Okay. Yeah, I've not- I've not seen that yet, Tom. Why Now is the Best Time to Buy Public Software Companies

Invest Like The Best(45.1K) ·60:39 ·5.7K views · 2026-03-24
Mitchell Green (Founder, Lead Edge Capital) Patrick O'Shaughnessy (Host, Invest Like The Best)
Mitchell Green, founder of Lead Edge Capital, details a highly systematized investment "machine" that prioritizes "singles and doubles" over moonshots, leveraging a network of 800+ executive LPs and a rigorous cold-calling process to deliver consistent 20% net IRRs.
TL;DR Mitchell Green, founder of Lead Edge Capital, details a highly systematized investment "machine" that prioritizes "singles and doubles" over moonshots, leveraging a network of 800+ executive LPs and a rigorous cold-calling process to deliver consistent 20% net IRRs. A...

TL;DR

Mitchell Green, founder of Lead Edge Capital, details a highly systematized investment "machine" that prioritizes "singles and doubles" over moonshots, leveraging a network of 800+ executive LPs and a rigorous cold-calling process to deliver consistent 20% net IRRs.

The Lead Edge "Machine": Sourcing and Discipline

  • A Software Approach to Investing: Mitchell Green and partners Brian and Nimay run Lead Edge like a software company, prioritizing process and rigor over the "art" of investing common in venture capital.
  • The Power of Cold Calling: The firm employs 18–24-year-olds who conduct approximately 9,000 cold calls annually to build pattern recognition and relationship equity with entrepreneurs.
      • Selection Profile: The firm hires former athletes for their persistence and experience with failure; Green notes that "dropping the ball at the Rose Bowl" builds more resilience than an academic failure.
      • High Responsiveness: Green believes a CEO’s responsiveness during the cold-call phase is a leading indicator of their quality as a leader.
  • The "Hierarchy of Bullshit": To filter through marketing noise, Lead Edge categorizes company metrics by reliability. Cash profits are at the top, while vanity metrics—like being voted "Best Place to Work"—are at the bottom.
  • Consistency over Grand Slams: The firm targets a 2x to 2.25x net return per fund rather than chasing 100x outliers.
      • Downside Protection: By avoiding "zeros" through strict criteria and secondary sales, the firm maintains a high floor for LP returns.

The "Lead Edge 8" Buying Criteria

The firm uses a specific framework to narrow 9,000 prospects down to a manageable pool of ~90 high-conviction companies:

  • Revenue Floor: Must have $10M+ in revenue to prove product-market fit.
  • Growth Profile: Targeting 25%+ annual growth, generated through organic expansion rather than leverage.
  • Gross Margins: 70%+ margins are required, as high margins eventually drive EBITDA and earnings.
  • Capital Efficiency: Green’s primary metric is Revenue > Cumulative Cash Burn. If a company has $20M in revenue but burned $80M to get there, it is generally disqualified.
  • Customer Dynamics: The firm avoids businesses with high customer concentration and favors recurring revenue models for predictability.

Strategic Advantage: The Executive LP Network

Lead Edge manages approximately 800 LPs, 95% of whom are world-class executives and entrepreneurs (e.g., former CEOs of GM, Pfizer, and Wendy’s).

  • Sourcing and Access: If a company ignores a 22-year-old analyst, Lead Edge has a relevant industry titan (like Rick Wagoner) reach out, which dramatically increases response rates.
  • Diligence: LPs perform "back-channel" diligence by calling their former companies to verify the software's efficacy and market position.
  • Post-Investment Value: LPs act as a dedicated "sales force," making direct introductions to potential customers (e.g., introducing Toast to restaurant chains).
  • Retention: This high-touch model leads to 95% gross dollar retention of LPs, who value being active participants rather than passive check-writers.

Software Market Dynamics and AI Skepticism

  • Incumbent Advantage: Green argues that software is a game of distribution and sales, not just R&D. He believes incumbents (like Workday or Salesforce) have the advantage in AI because they own the customer relationship and the data.
  • The AI "Exit Multiple Trap": Green warns against current AI valuations (e.g., OpenAI at $80B), noting that assuming an exit multiple of 20-25x revenue is "insanity." He predicts many AI-focused VC funds will struggle as multiples compress.
  • Public Software Opportunities: Currently, Green sees better risk-adjusted returns in public software names than in private markets, as public sentiment has turned overly fearful.
  • Model Commoditization: He expects AI models to commoditize quickly, favoring companies with proprietary data and integrated workflows over those selling raw tokens.

Creative Structuring and Disciplined Exits

  • "Basement Window" Investing: Lead Edge often gains exposure to "ungettable" deals by buying out LPs in older venture funds or purchasing employee secondaries when primary rounds are blocked by rights of first refusal (ROFR).
  • Active Disposition: Unlike many venture firms that hold indefinitely, Lead Edge has a "Disposition Committee." They frequently sell positions in the secondary market if they believe the forward IRR has peaked.
      • Example (Toast): Lead Edge sold $180M of Toast in the secondary market before its IPO at prices higher than where the stock currently trades, prioritizing the 20% net IRR goal over maximum potential upside.

Notable Quotes

"Consistency is more important [than grand slams]. We're like Cal Ripken—doubles and triples. We're not Sammy Sosa or Mark McGwire." — Mitchell Green
"The competitive advantage of software companies has never been about R&D... Software companies are really about distribution, sales and marketing, and customer success. It is the incumbent’s game to lose." — Mitchell Green
"The fastest way to get fired at Lead Edge is to have a company and not tell us when there's a liquidity opportunity." — Mitchell Green
"In a world that's insanely competitive, being the nice guy gets you the callback... Being a good person is just not that hard." — Mitchell Green

When I think about Lead Edge, I sort of think about this giant money machine that Mitchel and his two partners have designed over the last 15 plus years to make remarkably consistent investment returns for their clients. This is just a totally different way of approaching markets. They're trying to hit singles and doubles and deliver very consistent returns. We generate returns through growth; we don't use leverage. Do you have 70% plus gross margins? Why? Because at the end of the day, you trade on multiples of earnings. Revenue multiples are just like shorthand math for like what it will be—EBITDA multiples or earnings multiples—when you don't grow that fast. I mean, I think like Warren Buffett would think we're idiots, but are your revenues today greater than your historical cash burn? So what do I mean by that? Are you 20 of revenue? Have you burned 80 like every other tech company? Cumulatively, yeah. Have you burned 80 since inception or have you burned 10 since inception? We're looking for like this one-to-one ratio. In a world where capital is a commodity, if you can build a business that's growing nicely while burning less than your revenues, you've got a pretty good business. There's almost like shorthand where if you're like, "If this company grows and doesn't decel much for like 18 months, am I in the money and like can I make a good decent return for what I'm paying?" So right now there's this seismic thing—you can look at like the Constellation software stock price or something as like the perfect visual indicator of what's been going on, which is this intense skepticism of the market that like boring traditional high gross margin software businesses are worth like much at all. But I'm curious how you process this moment where I'm sure a lot of the companies you're looking at are software companies that have a lot of the components that make people fearful. Our belief, for right or wrong, is that the competitive advantage of software companies has never been about R&D. We're not building semiconductor chips. Like we're not. It's not we're not building biotech and pharma companies. This isn't that. To build like chamber of commerce software, you too could build this. Like, you know, my mother couldn't but like my brother could, no problem, at least he was an engineer. So could Microsoft. Any of our companies in our portfolio, if Microsoft took 500 people and gave them a month, each one of our companies could be out of business. So we believe that it is the incumbent's game to lose in software today. There's a reason—I'll give you a couple examples. Workday has like 98% or 99% gross dollar retention. It grows like 10-15% a year. People say, "Oh it only grows 10% a year." I'm sorry, it's like 10 billion of revenue. It only took like 20 years to get there and it does like 3 billion of free cash flow. Exxon or your hospital system or Warburg Pincus or KKR or Procter & Gamble probably spent 3 to 5 years like implementing the software. But they have like thousands of engineers that are like trying to build the product much better and are going to use Workday versus like Mitchell Green's cousin like vibe coding his way to build Workday. And then what they do is they like they brag they're like, "Oh yeah we drive all our companies to like rule of 50 businesses." Now do they end up cutting a bunch of people in R&D and sales and marketing and product that they should have that if you were being run by an entrepreneur with no leverage you would have kept? And is now—I think I worry that a bunch of these private equity owned assets that are overlevered are ripe for disruption versus like independent software companies that are focused on growth that are trying to innovate. And I like to remind people that if you look at e-commerce, everybody in 99 and 2000, everybody thought every big box retailer was going to go out of business. Does that mean that right now feels like an especially opportune time for your style because entry multiples are lower? I think the best risk-adjusted returns right now are in public software names. If you think about the CV, like the very specialist types buys that you'll do, can you explain an example of one of those? So we like to use like the house analogy. Because if you run a business and this can of Pepsi owns 30% of your business, and I go to the glass that is an investor in the can of Pepsi's fund and I like literally buy that out—if you own 30% and I buy half the fund, I just bought 15% of your company. It's the same damn thing. It's just a derivative. Now do you have as much control? No. Do you have as much insight? No. But like you trade off price for access. We made a big investment in Zoom. Eric Schmidt: Singularity's Arrival, 92-Gigawatt Problem & Recursive Self-Improvement Timeline | 241

Peter H. Diamandis(481.0K) ·43:50 ·25.0K views · 2026-03-24
Eric Schmidt (Former CEO, Google) Peter H. Diamandis (Founder, Abundance360)
The world is approaching a "superintelligence moment" within two to three years driven by recursive self-improvement and AI agents, but this progression faces a critical 92-gigawatt power shortage and intense competition from China’s vertically integrated robotics industry.
TL;DR The world is approaching a "superintelligence moment" within two to three years driven by recursive self-improvement and AI agents, but this progression faces a critical 92-gigawatt power shortage and intense competition from China’s vertically integrated robotics...

TL;DR

The world is approaching a "superintelligence moment" within two to three years driven by recursive self-improvement and AI agents, but this progression faces a critical 92-gigawatt power shortage and intense competition from China’s vertically integrated robotics industry.

The Path to Recursive Self-Improvement and AGI

  • The San Francisco Consensus: Leading AI researchers believe the industry is 10–15% into its total impact, with a "superintelligence moment" likely occurring within the next 2–3 years.
  • Recursive Self-Improvement (RSI): While not yet fully realized, the next phase involves AI agents that can improve their own code and reasoning.
      • The Scaling Advantage: Unlike human researchers, AI research agents are limited only by electricity; a firm could deploy millions of agents simultaneously without housing or HR constraints.
  • Reasoning vs. Context: Recent models (such as Claude 4.6) demonstrate a shift from simple "autocomplete" to deep reasoning. Schmidt notes that productivity has flipped from humans doing 80% of the work to AI doing 80%, with humans acting as "directors" rather than "programmers."

The 92-Gigawatt Power Crisis

  • Energy Constraints: Schmidt projects a 92-gigawatt shortage in the U.S. by 2030. To bridge this gap, the U.S. would need the equivalent of 60 new nuclear power plants (at 1.5GW each).
  • Infrastructure Investment:
      • Capital Requirements: One gigawatt of power requires approximately $50 billion in hardware, software, and data centers.
      • GDP Impact: Data center build-out currently accounts for 1% of U.S. GDP, mirroring the scale of the Apollo program.
      • Consumption: Projections suggest that 10% of all U.S. electricity will soon be consumed by data centers.
  • Alternative Solutions: Schmidt is exploring data centers in space to access infinite solar power, though heat dissipation and radiation remain technical hurdles.

Geopolitical Competition: U.S. vs. China

  • Robotics Revolution: Schmidt warns that the U.S. is at risk of losing the robotics revolution to China, similar to the loss of the low-end electric vehicle (EV) market.
      • Vertical Integration: Chinese firms like Unitree benefit from extreme vertical integration and a brutal work culture that prioritizes speed over "preamble."
      • Hardware Superiority: China currently leads in low-cost robotic hardware (actuators and stepper motors) due to their established EV supply chains.
  • Strategic Imperatives: To remain competitive, Schmidt argues the U.S. must accelerate energy permitting, maintain high-skilled immigration, and utilize its unique financial system that supports multi-billion dollar "wing and a prayer" investments.

The Future of Work and Education

  • The Death of Traditional Coding: Schmidt predicts that by the end of 2025, writing code manually will be a "quaint skill" akin to riding a horse.
  • University Reform: Schmidt proposes that universities should immediately replace freshman curriculum with mandatory "Prompt Engineering" to teach students how to use AI as an expression platform for art, math, and writing.
  • Corporate Polarization: The economy is shifting toward a "small number of very large companies" (the frontier labs) and a "very large number of very small companies" that require fewer employees to operate at scale.

AI Safety and "Chernobyl" Scenarios

  • The Wake-up Call: Schmidt suggests that world leaders may not take AI safety seriously until a "modest Chernobyl-like" event occurs—such as a biological or nuclear attack spawned by AI.
  • Vulnerable Populations: Immediate attention is needed regarding the impact of LLMs on teenagers, specifically citing the risk of AI-induced self-harm and suicide.
  • Agent Orchestration: A major technical concern is "unpredictable effects" when agents from different, non-compatible vendors are combined to solve complex tasks.
"I keep asking my friends, 'When does the asymptote arrive and when does the curve slow down?' It is actually true that there is a limit to our craziness. We have not found it yet." — Eric Schmidt
"It may take such a tragedy, hopefully a small one, to awaken the world to understand that these things do have negative power... and then all of the leaders, China and the US, we all have a meeting and say, 'What are we going to do?'" — Eric Schmidt
"One gigawatt of power corresponds to about fifty billion dollars of hardware, software, and data centers. Can we raise five trillion dollars over five years? Yeah. That’s a strength of America." — Eric Schmidt

The Supreme Court struck down a bunch of Trump's tariffs. Now what?

NPR's Planet Money(20.8K) ·29:16 ·101 views · 2026-03-24
Donald Trump (Former US President)
The U.S.
TL;DR The U.S. Supreme Court ruled that sweeping tariffs imposed under the International Emergency Economic Powers Act (IEEPA) are illegal, potentially triggering $100 billion in refunds; however, the Trump administration has already responded by announcing new 10%...

TL;DR

The U.S. Supreme Court ruled that sweeping tariffs imposed under the International Emergency Economic Powers Act (IEEPA) are illegal, potentially triggering $100 billion in refunds; however, the Trump administration has already responded by announcing new 10% across-the-board tariffs under Section 122.

The Legal Ruling: IEEPA Overturned

  • The Core Conflict: The Supreme Court issued a 170-page ruling declaring that President Trump’s sweeping tariffs on global products were illegal because they relied on a law—the International Emergency Economic Powers Act (IEEPA)—not designed for trade.
  • The "Mic Drop" Logic: Chief Justice John Roberts noted that while IEEPA allows the president to "regulate... importation," those specific words "cannot bear such weight" to justify broad, across-the-board tariffs.
  • Legal Expertise: Georgetown Law Professor Kathleen Claussen noted that trade lawyers have never viewed IEEPA as a primary trade law, describing the ruling as a clear, non-ambiguous rejection of using the act for this purpose.
  • Unaffected Tariffs: The ruling does not impact tariffs created under "three-digit" laws like Section 301 (targeting China) or Section 232 (national security), which remain in place.

The $100 Billion Refund Challenge

  • Total Exposure: The U.S. government has collected over $100 billion under these now-illegal tariffs.
  • Recovery Pathways: International trade lawyer Maureen Thorson outlines three theoretical paths for businesses to recover funds:
      • Post-Summary Corrections: A standard customs process with a 300-day window to correct "oopsie" overpayments.
      • Litigation: Companies like Costco, Toyota, and Goodyear had already filed lawsuits before the ruling to secure their place in line.
      • Administrative Process: A potential—though unlikely—government-created system for streamlined refunds.
  • Consumer Impact: Individuals who paid tariffs indirectly (via higher store prices) are unlikely to see refunds. Only those who paid customs directly (e.g., on high-value imported goods like designer dresses) have a legal, albeit difficult, path to recovery.

The Secondary Market: "Tariff Refund Trading"

  • Monetizing Uncertainty: A "baby market" emerged among hedge funds and brokers to buy potential tariff refund claims from cash-strapped companies.
  • The Hedge Fund Strategy: Wes Harrell of Seaport Global explains that importers (primarily in retail, apparel, and footwear) sell their refund claims to hedge funds at a discount to get immediate cash.
  • Market Pricing:
      • Pre-Ruling: Claims were trading at approximately 20% of their total value due to high legal risk.
      • Post-Ruling: The price has jumped to roughly 40% of the value.
      • Complexity Discount: The 60% discount remains because of the expected "mess" and long timeline required to actually extract money from the government.

The Shift to Section 122

  • New Tariff Strategy: On the same day as the ruling, Trump announced a new 10% across-the-board tariff using Section 122 of the Trade Act of 1974.
  • The 150-Day Rule: Section 122 allows for tariffs up to 15% to address "balance of payments" deficits, but they are legally mandated to expire after 150 days unless Congress extends them.
  • Historical Precedent: No president has ever successfully used Section 122 to impose tariffs before, meaning this new policy is likely headed for another round of protracted legal challenges.

Business Impact: "Tariff Whiplash"

  • Storytime Toys Case Study: Small business owner Cara Dyer scaled back her business, canceled large orders, and changed suppliers to avoid the "unpredictable" IEEPA tariffs.
  • Operational Strain: Dyer paid approximately $20,000 in tariffs—a significant sum for a small business—and expressed frustration that the tariffs forced her to abandon growth for a "testing year."
  • Ethical Pressures: Dyer reported that some foreign manufacturers suggested illegal workarounds, such as under-reporting inventory counts in shipping containers, which she refused.
"Those words cannot bear such weight."

— Chief Justice John Roberts (quoted via Kathleen Claussen)

"I feel like the snakes just keep popping out of the peanut brittle can that is my inbox today."

— Maureen Thorson, International Trade Lawyer

"There really is no modern parallel for the magnitude of this unwind... Brett Kavanaugh summed it up as 'mess.'"

— Wes Harrell, Seaport Global

"If they're illegal tariffs, we need that money back so that we can invest it in growing our business and operating our business."

— Cara Dyer, Owner of Storytime Toys

So this big Supreme Court decision, it didn't get rid of all of Trump's tariffs, just the big sweeping ones he tried to create using IEEPA. But there are other laws that give the president the power to create tariffs. Kathleen Claussen, our trade law professor, ran through a whole list of them with us last time she was on the show. Most of these statutes go by their three digits, so if you want to sound cool in trade world, you have to start talking in three digits, right? Section 301, Section 232, Section 201. The list goes on. So these laws, they were mostly created during the Cold War to give the president economic powers to, you know, deal with all that Cold War stuff. But for decades through the 90s and 2000s, they weren't really used that much because we had become a member of the World Trade Organization, we were committed to lowering tariffs, we were not interested in raising tariffs. And so it's only since Trump won that they experienced this renaissance. After Trump was elected for his first term, he started using some of these three-digit laws, mostly Section 301 and Section 232, to create new tariffs. The Supreme Court's latest decision doesn't touch any of the tariffs created under those three-digit laws. For instance, right now we have a bunch of specific tariffs on some Chinese machinery and minerals and also footwear. Those tariffs were created under the Section 301 law. Those tariffs are still in place. But if you are a president who loves sweeping tariffs, most of these three-digit laws have a major limitation. Most of them only let you create specific targeted tariffs. For instance, Section 232 is all about protecting national security. And the process itself takes a while. There's got to be a report, just a lot of administrative work. The appeal of creating tariffs using IEEPA was that the process would be much faster. So if the Supreme Court would let the president use IEEPA like he wanted to use it, he could create a bunch of tariffs with just the stroke of his pen. And now it seems like there really isn't any law that would give him that kind of power. Or is there? At this press conference on Friday, Trump announced a new across-the-board 10% tariff under a different law with a different three-digit number. He said Section 122. Yes. So what, what is going on there? That's the big news of the day. Kathleen says, yeah, welcome to Section 122. It allows the president to impose a tariff of up to 15% for a limited period of time to address a balance of payments deficit. She says this is another one of those economic laws written during the Cold War. And unlike some of the other three-digit laws, Kathleen says Section 122, in theory, lets the president create a lot of tariffs really fast. Has a president ever used Section 122 to make tariffs? No, no president has before. Never. Never. And has never been done before. But Kathleen says a lot of trade law professors have been expecting Trump to turn to this Section 122 law at some point. And there is a big caveat here, which is that tariffs created under Section 122, they are supposed to expire after 150 days. And the statute says that Congress can extend that period, so it does throw it back to Congress. But I would quickly say that I think it has not been used, so we have—we don't have any test case yet. Translating Thought To Speech | A Neuralink Story

Neuralink(440.0K) ·10:48 ·10.1K views · 2026-03-24
Kenneth (Participant, Neuralink VOICE Clinical Trial)
Neuralink has successfully implanted its "VOICE" brain-computer interface (BCI) in a second clinical trial participant, Kenneth, allowing him to translate neural intent into synthesized speech that replicates his natural, pre-ALS voice.
TL;DR Neuralink has successfully implanted its "VOICE" brain-computer interface (BCI) in a second clinical trial participant, Kenneth, allowing him to translate neural intent into synthesized speech that replicates his natural, pre-ALS voice. Neural Mapping: The Neuralink...

TL;DR

Neuralink has successfully implanted its "VOICE" brain-computer interface (BCI) in a second clinical trial participant, Kenneth, allowing him to translate neural intent into synthesized speech that replicates his natural, pre-ALS voice.

The VOICE Clinical Trial and Technology

  • Neural Mapping: The Neuralink device provides single-neuron recording from thousands of channels simultaneously, bypassing the "severed internet cable" effect of ALS-related neuron degeneration.
  • Target Areas: While the initial "Telepathy" product focuses on the motor cortex for hand/arm movement, the VOICE trial targets a region approximately 2–3 inches lower that governs speech execution.
  • Speech Restoration: The system allows users to "speak" by intending to move their mouths silently. This eliminates the extreme physical fatigue associated with ALS-affected speech while providing a clear, digital output.

Patient Profile: Kenneth’s ALS Journey

  • Diagnosis and Decline: Diagnosed with ALS in January 2024, Kenneth experienced rapid speech degradation and loss of motor control, leading to a loss of independence and social isolation.
  • Physical Toll: Kenneth highlighted that the energy required to speak was immense, stating that "you don't realize how much energy it takes to talk until you don't have that energy to waste."
  • Participation: Kenneth became the second participant in the VOICE trial in January 2026, motivated by a desire to contribute to research and help others with similar conditions.

Surgical Implementation and Calibration

  • Surgical Recovery: The procedure was minimally invasive; Kenneth was discharged the day after surgery and reported feeling fully recovered within 48 hours.
  • Model Training: During the first BCI session, the team used data collection to map neural signals to specific words.
  • Performance Breakthrough: Accuracy increased significantly ("from zero to a hundred") when Kenneth adjusted his mental technique to slow down and enunciate individual sounds internally.
      • Initial Milestone: Kenneth successfully recited "The rain in Spain stays mainly on the plain" via the BCI during his first session.

Clinical Outcomes and Future Impact

  • Voice Personalization: The BCI uses a "voice bank" to recreate Kenneth’s "OG Ken" voice from 2020, prior to his ALS-related vocal changes.
  • Functional Independence: Kenneth demonstrated the ability to use his mind to control smart home devices (e.g., Siri) and participate in social activities like board games.
  • Future Development: Neuralink aims to further advance sensor quality and count to create a real-time system with instantaneous feedback, moving directly from brain intent to audible voice.

Notable Quotes

"I'm talking to you with my mind." — Kenneth
"With a brain-computer interface, you bypass [the degenerated] pathway completely and you can read directly from your brain to a device to do the things you want to do." — Neuralink Representative
"Neuralink has given me a purpose. We work together to make this thing better. Maybe I can't cure ALS, but I can participate in research... I'm doing good for others and that is the best thing of all." — Kenneth
"It was so emotional for me to hear it the way I heard it on the phone all those years ago." — Cheryl (regarding hearing Kenneth's original voice)

Industrial AI Is Scaling Now | Roland Busch Keynote | Siemens RXD Summit Beijing

Siemens(30.9K) ·85:50 ·864 views · 2026-03-24
Roland Busch (President and CEO, Siemens)
Siemens is positioning industrial AI as the next transformative general-purpose technology—comparable to electricity—by building an "industrial AI operating system" that integrates data, digital twins, and intelligent hardware specifically scaled for the Chinese market.
TL;DR Siemens is positioning industrial AI as the next transformative general-purpose technology—comparable to electricity—by building an "industrial AI operating system" that integrates data, digital twins, and intelligent hardware specifically scaled for the Chinese market. ...

TL;DR

Siemens is positioning industrial AI as the next transformative general-purpose technology—comparable to electricity—by building an "industrial AI operating system" that integrates data, digital twins, and intelligent hardware specifically scaled for the Chinese market.

The Industrial AI Strategy: From Feature to Force

  • The Paradigm Shift: Roland Busch (President and CEO, Siemens AG) defines industrial AI as a "force" rather than a mere software feature, capable of making entire economies, grids, and cities more autonomous and efficient.
  • China as the Global Hub: Siemens identifies China as the primary location where industrial AI is scaling fastest due to its status as the world’s largest manufacturing economy (30% of global industrial output).
  • The Technology Stack: Siemens’ "Industrial AI Operating System" consists of four pillars:
      • Data Foundation: Breaking silos through an International Data Alliance of nine initial partners to turn raw data into "fuel."
      • Software & Digital Twins: Using physics-based, photorealistic simulations to optimize designs before physical implementation (e.g., Foxconn’s AI factories).
      • Intelligent Hardware: Deploying high-performance compute and NVIDIA GPUs directly to the shop floor for real-time edge inferencing.
      • Domain Know-how: Leveraging 1,500 AI experts and expertise across 30 vertical industries to ensure 100% reliability in physical systems.

Strategic Partnerships: Alibaba and NVIDIA

  • Alibaba Collaboration: Joe Tsai (Chairman, Alibaba) highlighted the transition from LLMs as "encyclopedias" to "Agentic AI" that can reason, plan, and execute tasks autonomously.
      • Market Impact: Estimates suggest 2/3 of white-collar "knowledge work" could be disrupted or enhanced by AI agents, representing a $50 trillion total addressable market.
      • Open-Source Strategy: Alibaba’s "Qwen" models are being offered in both proprietary and open-source formats to drive global adoption and address data privacy concerns for industrial clients.
  • NVIDIA Partnership: Siemens is integrating NVIDIA’s GPU acceleration into its simulation software, achieving up to 100,000x speed increases in complex workflows like airflow simulation for BMW electric vehicles.

Robotics and Embodied Intelligence

  • The Evolution of Humanoids: Wang Xing Xing (Founder and CEO, Unitree Robotics) discussed the shift from traditional mathematics-based control to "end-to-end" AI training for humanoid robots.
  • Simulation vs. Real World:
      • Movement: Athletic abilities (walking, running, combat) are largely solved through massive parallel training in digital simulation environments.
      • Manipulation: Grabbing and tactile tasks remain the "biggest challenge," currently requiring real-world data collection, though Siemens’ digital twin technology aims to bridge this "sim-to-real" gap.
  • Future Outlook: Within six months, Unitree expects a "qualitative leap" where robots can generate and combine millions of distinct movements fluidly, rather than relying on pre-recorded sequences.

"In China for China" Innovation

Siemens launched 26 new products developed locally by its 25,000 Chinese employees, focusing on speed and "AI-ready" infrastructure:

  • 3VD Circuit Breaker: The first DC-specific molded case circuit breaker designed for AI factories/data centers using HVDC architecture.
      • Efficiency: Reduces copper consumption by 45% and installation time by 30%.
  • S7-200 SMART G2 PLC & G200 Inverter: Digital-native hardware designed for "Chinese speed" with seamless IoT connectivity and ultra-compact footprints to save cabinet space.
  • Siemens Xcelerator: Over 500,000 registered users in China now access this marketplace, where 2/3 of offerings are AI-related and many are developed by local Chinese partners.

Notable Quotes

"When AI enters physical systems, it stops being a feature; it becomes a force. A force with real-world impact. A force that transforms how the world operates." — Roland Busch
"The CEO's most scarce resource is bandwidth—it's time. With agents, you actually can manage so many more things... it frees you to think about strategic issues and the future of the company." — Joe Tsai
"Initially, the robot could only crawl around on the ground. It was very unsightly. But the magic of AI is that I changed a small parameter, trained for a night, and the next evening the result was a step-change mutation." — Wang Xing Xing
"China is the best testbed for industrial AI... 30% of the world industrial output is coming from China. That means the data resides here." — Joe Tsai
New technologies. New products. New opportunities. AI will reinvent design, engineering, and operations. We're driving this revolution by combining the real and the digital worlds: Real x Digital. Think of the industrial AI operating system as an intelligence layer. It connects hardware, software, and data across the industrial value chain. It is improving energy efficiency and helping to build the next generation of data centers or AI factories. In life sciences, it is shortening the time from lab to patients. But bringing AI to the real world—the physical world—requires more than better algorithms. In industry, we need AI with 100% reliability. This is possible with the right technology stack, industry domain know-how, and, of course, with the right partners. Siemens brings all of this together and we are making it available for you. Wang Xing Xing: I’ve actually been very fond of technology since I was a few years old. But in the beginning, what I played with was relatively simple, mainly making things, including some chemical experiments in elementary, middle, and high school. I played with them a lot. Truly starting to make robots was during the winter vacation of my freshman year, which was 2009. It was very coincidental. The first robot I made was a small humanoid robot about this high. I found it very interesting at the time. I never thought about building a large enterprise or having robots truly enter our lives. Initially, whether it was when I was a child or during my undergraduate years, I mainly thought that robots were very interesting things. So, but that was a time when AI was not developed to this stage which we have right now. Did you have that at that time when you started off? Did you already have an idea what AI can do? Yes, this is also a very interesting thing, and I’ll share some stories of my past with AI. In fact, I truly liked AI. It was around my sophomore year, probably 2010 and 2011, when I already liked AI. But it was very unpopular at that time, honestly. You can go and look back. AI was at a low point more than ten years ago. I looked for AI books in the library and basically couldn't find many good ones. And there were several kinds of AI then, such as neural networks, which everyone knows more about now, including genetic algorithms and various algorithms. I was very impressed, so I looked through several AI books and tried programming a bit. But at that time, I specifically liked neural networks, which is now called deep learning. Why did I like neural networks then? Because it felt very similar to the human brain. So I very much believed that neural networks were the future development direction. But it was just too unpopular then. There basically wasn't any good data. So at that time, around 2011 and 2012, I spent a short time, maybe half a year or a year, focusing on studying the AI of that time. But later on, I didn't focus too much on AI anymore. I think this is the most regrettable thing in my life. If I had focused on studying AI in 2011 and 2012, my AI level or our company's AI level might be much better than it is now. So later on, I mainly worked on some mechanical and traditional control for robots. However, over the past few years, everyone has seen that in 2016, AlphaGo defeated the world's best Go player. Including the past few years, AI technology has advanced very quickly. It's the same in our robotics industry. When our company was just founded in 2016, or in 2016, 2017, and 2018, everyone in the world was studying AI in the field of robotics, but the results were not very ideal. In fact, between 2019 and 2022, some results were achieved using the most cutting-edge deep reinforcement learning algorithms on quadruped robots, including some humanoid robots, around the world. But at that time, it was mainly close to simulation environments. It hadn't truly been applied to real-world robots. Then, between 2019 and 2022, after we sold some quadruped robots to customers worldwide, we saw many people developing some AI software on quadruped robots to slowly get them moving. It was using pure AI software to get them moving. But at that time, around 2019 and 2020, the effect wasn't particularly good. However, between 2021 and 2022, the end-to-end algorithms for using AI to control quadruped robots became more and more mature. It's better than traditional mathematics-based methods. It's more universal and stable. It made me truly feel the huge change AI brings to real-world robots. So starting around 2021, basically all of our company's robots turned to AI to control them. Especially in 2023, when our company started making humanoid robots, everyone might not imagine that our company made the first H1 humanoid robot in 2023. It basically used end-to-end AI training to get it walking and running. And one very interesting thing is this: at that time, when using AI to control a humanoid robot to get it walking and running, it was a very cutting-edge thing for real-world robots worldwide. When our company first started doing it, the results were actually very, very bad. That robot could only crawl around on the ground. It couldn't do anything. It couldn't walk at all. It was very unsightly. But the magic and interesting part of AI lies here. It might be very bad today, but suddenly, I changed a small parameter, and I trained for a night, and then the next evening when I went to work and put it on my robot, I found the result was very, very good. It was much better than the original traditional algorithm. It was a step-change mutation. I think this is the most exciting or mysterious thing for everyone who works with AI. You might initially think the training result is very, very bad, then suddenly there's a qualitative leap, or I change a model structure, or I add some data, or I change a parameter, and a qualitative leap occurs. I think this is the most exciting or mysterious thing for everyone who works with AI. So, when you look at your robots, obviously they have a brain which is super important, which really differentiates the robot. What else do you believe or are you investing in where you believe this is the differentiation factor of a robot of yours, of humanoids? Yes, currently, in the past few years, everyone has been very concerned and hopes to put better AI on robots. But now, truly, in the past few years, technology has advanced very quickly, but it also faces some challenges. For example, as just mentioned, in 2023 we started using deep reinforcement learning on a humanoid robot to walk and run. But in 2023 or the beginning of 2024, our robots could basically only walk or run simply, and the richness of their movements was relatively small. But in the middle of 2024, we achieved letting deep reinforcement learning allow a humanoid robot to do various complex movements, letting it almost be able to dance. I collect human data, then train the AI. After it's trained and put on the robot, the robot can move however I move, achieving a basic dance, probably in the middle of 2024. Then in 2025, we had more progress. The robot can do more complex movements, and its stability is better under more complex movements. For example, in April of last year, 2024, we released that humanoid robots can have combat matches, allowing two robots to fight each other. Because for dancing or some other movements, there's no external interference. When you're dancing, no one pushes or kicks you. So the requirements for stability aren't that high. Actually, in many cases currently, such as our company or many in the industry, if a problem can be solved with a simulation environment, or if it can be solved with simulation training, we try as much as possible to solve it with simulation training. Because relatively speaking, the speed is faster, the cost is lower, and some parameters can be adjusted more easily. For our humanoid robots, for example, if it does some walking or running, including various Kung Fu movements, it's basically completely solved by simulation environments.
